and so sīos, mar ghnāthuighear a n-Eagluisibh Alban
do ghrādhuigh agas do ghlac Soisgēl Dīleas Dé,
tar ēis an fhuar-chreidimh do chur ar gcūl;
arna dtarraing as Laidin agas as Gaill-Bhērla					10
i nGaoidheilg lē

M. SEON CARSUEL

Ministir Eagluise Dé a gcrīochaibh Earra Gaoidheal,
darab comh-ainm Easbug Inndseadh Gall.

Nī h-éidir lē h-ēn-duine fundamuint oile do					15
tsuidhiughadh acht an fhundamuint atā
arna suighiughadh .i. Iosa Crīosd. I Cor. 3.

DON TRIATH CHUMHACHTACH cheirt-
bhreathach chiūin-bhriathrach, do Ghiolla
Easbuig Ua nDuibhne, Iarrla Earra Gaoidheal agas
Tighearna Ladharna agas Ard-ghiúisdis na h-Alban,				25
darab comh-ainm Lieutenanda a gcrīochaibh Oilēn
Indseadh Gall, agas Aird-cheand teaghluigh na
rīghruidhe Albanuidhe, atā M. Séon Carsuel, Minis-
tir tSoisgēil Dé ag guidhe agas ag gér-atach Dé go
dīochra dūthrachtach fā spioraid an ghlicais agas				30
na tuigse agas na fīrinde do neartughadh agas do
mhēdughadh and, maille rē sonas suthain d’fhaghāil
dó, ō Dhia Athar tré Iosa Crīosd ar dTighearna.

NOS OIRRDHEIRC ONORACH atā fós agas do bhī			[A2v
riamh ar buil ag na daoinibh ghnāthuigheas saothur nō			35
obuir do dhēnamh, do chum an tsaothair-sin do dhul a
dtarbha dona poibleachaibh agas dona cineadhachaibh agas
dā sliocht agas dā lorg ina ndiaigh, toradh an t-saothair-sin
do thiodhlucadh dona tighearnaibh nō dona cairdibh nō
dona cumpānachaibh rēra cosmhula an saothar-sin fēin, agas		40
is fearr d’airidhe air, agas is mó ar a mbī cumaoin ghrāidh
fhir dhēnta an t-saothair.

Atāim-se, a thriath tairise agas a thighearrna dingmhālta,
ag leanmhuin an nósa-so go feadh mo chumhachta; agas agā
thuigsin cosamhlacht an adhbhair-se rē t’indtind dhiadha-sa,		45
agas th’fheabhus d’airidhe ar dheagh-thiodhlucadh, agas
méd do chomaoine grāidh imarcaidh oram, agas ar an adh-
bhur-sin, do condcus damh-sa gurbh in-dēnta damh an
saothar beag-sa, neoch is cēd-toradh dom intleacht, do
thoirbheart agas do thiodhlucadh duit-se, a thighearrna.			50
Oir do dhearbh do bheatha dhi/adha-sa ō aois do leanbachta			[A3
dúinne go bhfuil an t-adhbhar diadha-sa cosmhuil rit, agas


25: tíghearna.
26: Liuetenanda.

EBISTIL THIOGHLAICTHE

fós do dhearbh truime an eire do ghabhais ort ag cathughadh
a n-adhaidh na druinge lērbh āil an soisgēl naomhtha do
chur ar gcūl th’fheabhas d’airidhe air gan sin do dhul ar			55
neifnī choidhche, agas gan t’ainm oirrdheirc-se do dhul ar
dearmad go deireadh an domhain.

Agas gē tāid nāimhde na fīrinde go mī-nāireach agā
rāgha nach dlighfedīs na prindsadha nō na tighearrnaidhe
saoghalta cūrum do bheith orrtha a dtimcheall hsoisgēil Dé,			60
nō a dtimcheall dhroch-chreidimh do sgris, is fearr do thuig
tusa an fāidh naomhtha ina bhriathraibh inā mur sin .i.
Dāibhīth, neoch iarus ar prindsaghaibh na talmhun beith
eōlach neamh-ainbfeasach sa ladh dhiadha. Is mur sin
fuaradar na sean-aithreacha diadha (sul do sgrībhadh an			65
ladh) saoghal fada, agas tiodhluicthe maithe eile, do chum
iad fēin do bheith eōlach a dtoil Dē, agas do chum go / ndēn-		[A3v
daois an toil-sin Dé do theagasg dā gclandaibh agas dā
sleachtaibh ina ndiaidh. Agas gē do-rindeadh mēid ēigin do
dhealughadh eidir oifig Mhaoise agas oifig Aróin an uair			70
tugadh an ladh do Chlandaibh Israhēl sgrībhtha, gidh eadh
dearbhthar dhúinn lē a mbrāithreas, agas leis an gcūrum do
ghabh Maoisi air, nach amhāin tug sē aire dona neithibh
saoghalta, acht gurab mó an aire do bhī aige ar an ladh
dhiadha do chongbhāil gan truailleadh a measg an phobail			75
iná ar na neithibh saoghalta. Agas foillsighthear sin dúinn
go maith leis an achmhusān tug sé d’Arōn agas don phobal
uile, ag faigsin na h-onōra tugadar don laodh órdha do-
rindeadar a n-adhaidh thoile Dé, an uair do bhris seisean an
dā thabhaill ina rabhadar na deich n-aitheanta sgrībhtha ō			80
mhēr Dē na n-uile chumhacht, agas do-rinde Maoise dīo-
ghaltas ro-throm orrtha as sin.

Do thuigeadar na breitheamhain agas na rīghthe deagh-
chreidmheacha do bhī ar Chloind Israhēl an nī-se do labh-
ramar / romhaind, mar do bhī Geidion, agas Samuel, agas			[A4
Dāibhīth, agas Isafat, agas Esecias, agas Iosias, agas cuid
eile do na breitheamhnaibh agas dona rīghaibh ar a bfuil
masla agas toibhēim a dtimna Dhé ō dheachtadh an Sbirad
Naoimh. Asē adhbhar fā dtarrla dhāibh bheith fān athais
agas fān imdheargadh-sin ō Dhia, nār thuigeadar go mbean-			90

gcreiddīs, agas dona feallsamhnaibh, agas dona daoinibh

<eng>Pugnabo pro templis et sacris, solus et cum aliis<gai> .i. Cathōchaidh

deamh ag an phoball gheintlighe go h-uilidhe dā ndēibh			115
agas dā dteampluibh. Dā rēir sin is follas go bfuillid an
mhuintear iarras ar tighearnaibh agas ar prindsaghaibh
Crīsduidhe gan cúrum an reachta diadha do bheith orra ina
nāimh/dibh don nādūir dhaona.						[A5]

Is andsa timna-sa Iosa atā gach uile nī rigmaoid a leas			120
dār dteagasg. Atā indte an ladh, neoch nochtus dúind mar

maitheamh a pheacadh ar gach ēn-duine aithridheach, óir			125
is andsa lē Dia aithridhe an pheacaidh inā a bhās. Agas atā
indte oidhreacht fhlaitheamhnuis Dé, arna gealladh dā gach
neach ghabhas Crīosd ar dTighearna chuige ’na chroidhe

maille rē deagh-chreideamh. Mās eadh, cindus do múchadh
agas do bāitheadh agas do truailleadh an timna-sa Chrīsd;			130
cindus do sgriosadh a ladh lē foirrneart an Phāpa agas na
n-easbul bfallsa eile, neoch do mheall an saoghal go h-uilidhe
isin aimsir-se lē n-ainbfios imarcach, is follus duit-se, a
thigherna. Oir nī h-air dhīmhaoineas do-chuaidh dhuit an
saothar do-rinde tū as t’ōige ag lēghadh an sgribtūir dhiadha;		135
/ oir is mō do chuir tū a suim an nī do dhearbh an soisgēl			[A5v]
diadha dhuit inā mēid oirrdhearcais th’aoisi, agas fad an
ghnāthuighe do-chualais do bheith ag na sindsearaibh onō-
racha do-chuaidh romhad, ag aithris ar an rīgh dhiadha
Esecias, neoch do bhī ’na ógānach ar bfaghāil rīgheachta dó.		140
Aseadh do-rinde sé, an leabhar diadha do ghabhāil ’na lāimh
.i. an timna ina bfuil toil Dé, agas a foillsiughadh dona poi-
bleachaibh, agas tug ar na fāidhibh agas ar na sagartaibh

h-indarbadh leis an ladh truaillidhe tugadar na sagairt			145
shantacha a n-inadh reachta Dé, do chomhmōradh agas do
mhēdughadh a dtighearrntuis tsaoghalta féin le h-ainbfis a
ndīmhaoinis ar comhairle tSatain .i. an ainsbiraid, nāmha
rīghachta Crīst, gan fhēchain do dhochar dhā mhēd dá bfēd-
fadh techt i ndiaidh an adhbhuir-sin.						150

Nīr fhēch Maoisi dā chuntabhairt an uair do ghabh sé do
lāimh, ar furāileamh Dé, Cland Israhēl do thabhairt as an
Eigeift, / agas ard-mhonadh nach bfētfuidhe a dhul ar gach			[A6]
taobh dībh, agas Faró lē neart a tsluaigh uile ’na ndiaidh.
Nīr fhēch Geidion do loidhed a mhuintire an uair do indsuidh		155
an sluagh geinntlidhe lē trī chéd, agas nīorbh ēidir a raibhe
’na adhaidh d’āireamh ar a n-imad. Nīr fhēch Dāibhīoth
rīgh, agas é ’na ōgānach ar dteacht ó aodhuireacht a thrēda,
do loighead a chuirp féin nō dhá ōige nō dhá neamh-fhoir-
bhtheacht a gceardaibh gaisgidh nō eangnamha, gan dul do			160
chomhrac rē Goilias, fomhoir uathmhur ain-mīn an-arrachta,
ro-mhōr ō chorp, foirfe a n-aois, agas é eōlach intleachtach
a gcathaibh agas a gcomhlandaibh; agas gidh eadh, do chind
leō na h-oibreacha agas na saothair-sin do ghabhadar do
lāimh a h-ucht an Sbirad Naoimh, agas ar aithne Dhé na			165
n-uile chumhacht.

Do chind leis an rīgh ro-mhaith do rāidheamar romhaind
.i. Esachias, sgris na ndealbh agas na mbileadh, agas briseadh
na naithreach prāis. Do chind a obair agas a eachtra lē /			[A6v]
Maoise ag tabhairt an phobail trēsan bfairrge, agas agā			170
mbeathughadh san dīthreibh rē dā fhithead bliadhan leis an
manda .i. leis an mbeathaidh tugadh dō do neimh, tar ēis an
rīgh agas na n-Eigifteach uile do bhāthadh. Do chind lē
Gedion briseadh ar a nāimhdibh agas ar a eascairdibh acht
gēr bheg a mhuintear ag fēchain a raibhe ’na adhaidh. Do			175
chind le Dāibhīth, agas é ’na mhacāmh óg an-arrsaigh,
Golias neartmhar neimh-meirbh do mharbhadh d’ēn-urchur
cloiche as a chrand-tabhaill.

Do chind leat-sa, a thighearna, ar aithris na rīghruidhe
ro-uaisle sin do rāidheamar romhaind, gach obair agas gach			180
ard-tsaothar dār thinnsgain tū, tré rath an Sbirad Naomh
agas lē h-eōlas an sgribhtūir dhiadha, gan fhēchain do
chundtabhairt nō do ghábhadh nō do ghuasacht dā raibhe
oraibh .i. sgris fuar-chreidimh agas fuar-chrādhbhaidh, agas
losgadh dealbh agas droch-eisimlāra, agas leagadh agas lān-		185
bhriseadh altōr agas inodh a ndēntaoi iodhbarta / brēige do			[A7]
bhunadh, agas sgris meirleach agas mī-bhēsach agas [lucht]
foghla agas foirēigne, agas ’na dhiaidh sin mēdughadh agas
mór-chumhdach agas onōrughadh na h-Eagluisi Crīsduidhe
go comhlān. Oir is buaine dhuit an caithrēim-si a bfiadhnuisi		190
Dé inā gach caithrēim saoghalta oile, mar atā creachadh
agas comh-lomadh na gcomharsand agas na gcoigcrīch, agas
marbhadh agas mór-mhughughadh a ndaoine, agas losgadh
agas lán-mhilleadh a n-aitreabh agas a n-āras.

Agas gidh eadh, ní fhuil a n-ainbfis damh-sa go mbēraid			195
mōrān do na h-easbulaibh fallsa agas dona daoinibh atā ar
seachrān creidimh fós breath a n-adhaidh na mbriathar-sa
adubhramar romhaind, agas adēraid gurab cogadh agas
argain ar an eagluis, agas fós gurab sgris ar an tuaith an
obair do ghabhais do lāimh; agas fós gurab droch-chreidimh		200
agas gurab teagasg gan tarbha an soisgēl atāmaoid do noch-
tadh dōibh. Agas gidh eadh, adeirim-si gan chuntabhairt
dā rēir sin go bfuilit-sean féin ag leanmhuin / luirg a sindsear,		[A7v]
ag tabhairt tighearndtuis agas umhla don reacht dhaonda

EBISTIL THIOGHLAICTHE

inar beathuigheadh iad, agas ag tabhairt fhuatha don reacht			205
dhiadha dhligheas gach aon-duine do ghrādhughadh, agas
go dtarrla an mhēid-si do mhī-nāire do dhruing aca go
dtugadar freiteach rē soisgēl Dé dh’ēisteacht agas go n-abruid
an Bībla naomhtha do bheith brēgach, mar adubhradar na
h-Iūbhail lé Crīsd ar dTighearna: <eng>Seductor populi daemonium<gai>		210
<eng>habens<gai> .i. Is cealguire an phobail é agas atā deamhan and;
agas mar adubhradar Cland Israhēl go minic rē Maoise agas
ris na fāidhibh eile go raibhe siad ag buaidhreadh na bpoi-
bleach, ar son a bheith dhōibh ag nochtadh aithentadh agas
thoile Dé, agas ar son bheith dhā iarraidh orra a ndēe fallsa			215
agas a ndealbha brēige do thrēigean. Asē an t-adhbhar cēdna
atā anois ag an t-saoghal bhuaidheartha dhaoibh-se, a thi-
ghearna, agas dúinne. Gidh eadh, is tuicthe dhuit-se, a
thighearna, agas dúinne fós, nach oircheas slighe mhaith nō
adhbhur maith do / thrēigean ar son chothaighe agas fhuatha		[A8]
na ndroch-dhaoine adēradh sin.

Ar an adhbhur-sin dob áil linde an ní nach gcualamar do
dhēnamh romhaind do thindsgna anois, mar atá foirm agas
brīdh na n-urrnaidheadh agas na sacramuinteadh naomhtha
do chur i nGaoidheilg; óir do ghabhamar an mhéid-sin do			225
mheisnigh chugaind ar ndītheall do dhēnamh, a mbriathruibh
Crīosd, do chomhfhurtacht na h-Eagluise Crīsdaidhe. Acht
gé nach ro-mhaith ar nGaoidhealg, gidh eadh is gabhtha mo
dheagh-thoil uaim-se ar son mh’uireasbhadha, agas adbhuim
féin fós uireasbhuidh mhór do bheith oram a gcanamhain			230
Ghaoidheilge agas a bfoirm mo dheachtaidh, agas ar an
adhbhur-sin geallaim umhla do thabhairt don tí is eōlcha
iná mé. Acht cheana, atá a fhis agam nach a milis-bhriath-
ruibh na bfeallsamh do cuireadh an sgriobhtūir diadha, agas
nach bfuil feidhm aige ar dhath breadhdha brēgach na			235
bfileadh do chur air. Oir is lór don fhírinde í féin mar
fhiadhnuise, gan brat oile do chur impe, acht gé tā feidhm
ag an mbrēig tsao/ghalta ar dhath égsamhail oile do chur			[A8v]
impe dā cumhdach don taobh amuigh, ar an adhbhar go
bfuil sī lochtach don taobh astoigh. Agas gē bé dā dtug			240
Dia do thidhlocadh bheith eōlach a gceart chanamhna

EBISTIL THIOGHLAICTHE

sgrībhaidh agas deachtaidh agas labhartha na Gaoidheilge,
is mó atá d’fhiachaibh air an tiodhlucadh-sin fuair sé ó Dhia
do chaitheamh ré cumhdach agas ré comhmōradh na fīrinde
atá a soisgēl Dé, iná a chaitheamh ré cumhdach brēige nó			245
ēgōra nó seachráin a n-adhaidh an t-soisgēil-sin.

An t-adhbhar prindsibālta do bhrosnaidh mhé do chum
an t-saothair-se do dhénamh .i. grádh Dé ar tús, agas ar eagla
go n-aigeōradh sé oram an tálenta .i. an tidhlucadh tug sé
dhamh do chur ar dimbuil, agas grádh Eagluise Dé, agas do			250
dhénamh cuidighe lēm bhrāithribh Crīosdaidhe atá a bfeidhm
theagaisg agas chomhfhurtachta, agas ar a bfuil díth leabhar.
Agas maille ris na h-adhbharaibh-sin, do bhrosnaidh go mór
mhé méd mo dhóchais as do dhaingne-si agas as do bhuaine
sa slighe dhiadha do ghlacais as t’ōige agas as t’anfhoir-			255
feacht, / a thighearna, ag breathnughadh agas ag tuigsin			[B
gurab pátrūn dīleas dingmhālta agas gurab oide carthanach
don fhīrinde thú, agas fós gurab cádhas agas comairce do na
daoinibh anbfanda ēgcruaidhe bhīos i ngábhadh nō i
nguasacht ar sgāth na fīrinde thú; agas tuigmaoid fós gurab			260
athair don chloind bhīs ar athchur agas ar indarbadh ar son
na fírinde thú; agas fós go bfēdaid timpiridhe agas teach-
tuiridhe agas minisdrighe na fīrinde comhnuidhe agas comh-
thoiriseamh do dhēnamh fād sgiathānaibh.

Agas atāmaoid agā rādha ribh, a thighearna, gan simpli-			265
dheacht an tidhluice nō fhir an tidhluice do mheas, acht
feabhas na sligheadh agas an adhbhair, agas grādh agas
gnāth-umhla neamh-chealgach fhir an tidhluice dhuit do
mheas. Oir dā mbeind-se a gcomhthrom ghlicais agas eōlais

cuimhne ’na gcroidheadhaibh. Agas adubhairt an Tighearna			565

DO THOGHA NA MINISDREADH

maithe dearbhtha deagh-choing/hiollacha do thabhairt a			[C7]
bfiadhnaise adhbhair an mhinistir agas a fhēchain an bfuil
sé eōlach eagnaidhe cumhachtach ar an Sgriobhtūir dhiadha
do thuigsin agas do chur a gcēill do chāch, agas an a bfuilid			710
aige na tiodhlaice agas na coinghill nochtas an Sgriobhtūir
don phobal. Agas tabharthar an Sgriobhtūir féin chuige a
n-ionad uaigneach agas ēistear a bhriathra agas a theagasg
and sin, agas dēntar a bhreitheamhnas dā rēir sin.

An dara ní, gabhaid sgēla a bheatha agas a bhuan-				715
ámhaille ’nar chaith sé a ndeachaidh thairis dá aimsir gan
guth gan toibhēim gan mī-chlū d’fhaghāil, agas dá rabh sé
dearbhtha in-mholta mar sin isna neithibh adubhramar
romhaind, tugaid lucht a thogha cairrde go ceand aimsire
dhó nō go ma dearbhtha deimhin lé cách a bheith deigh-			720
bhēsach dhó.

Agas ’na dhiaidh sin iarradh an ministir ar an phobal iad
féin do lēigean a n-ísle agas a n-umhla do Dhia ré trosgadh
agas ré h-urrnuidhe, agas a iarraidh ar Dia an / togha sin do			[C7v]
dhul a dtarbha agas a dtābhacht don eagluis. Agas dā			725
bfaghthar én-locht ré chur ’na adhaidh ō ladh nó ó dhligh-
eadh maith, lēicthear siubhal dó agas cuirthear neach oile go
h-imchubhaidh ’na ionadh. Agas mar faghthar locht ar
bioth ré chur ’na adhaidh beirthear do chum na searmōna
maidne é a bfiadhnaise na h-eagluise, agas beanadh cuid			730
éigin don t-searmōin-sin ré togha an mhinistir féin.

Agas ’na dhiaidh-sin tar ēis an mheadhóin laoi, i ndeir-
eadh na searmōna, brosnuidheadh an ministir iad do chum
na togha, ar tús ag eadar-ghuidhe Dé na n-uile chumhacht
amhail do theagaisg an Sbiorad Naomh a chroidhe do			735
dhēnamh urrnuidhe. Agas mar an gcēdna i ndiaidh na togha
tugadh an ministir buidheachas do Dhia, ag cuimhniughadh
a oifige do rēir mar toghthar é.

Agas i ndiaidh na togha marsin moladh an pobal Dia ré
salm éigin sul sgaoilfeas siad ó cheile./					740

D’OIFIG NA BFOIRFIDHEACH

DONA FOIRFIDHEACHAIBH AGAS DA N-OIFIGE			[C8]
agas dā dtogha and so sīos.

Dlighid na foirfidhe bheith ’na ndaoinibh diadha deigh-
bheathadh, agas gan adhbhar amharais do bheith orra, agas
cūram an tréda ó ghliocas do bheith orra, agas a gceand gach		745
uile neithe eagla Dhé go mōr do bheith orra. Agas así so a
n-oifige: bheith ag riaghladh agas ag sdiūradh agas ag cear-
tughadh agas ag ordughadh gach neithe bheanas ré sdaid
mhaith na h-Eagluise maille ris na minisdribh. Agas asé
dealughadh atá eidir siad agas na minisdrighe, nach dēnaid			750
na foirfidhe searmōin nō freasdal na sacramuinte. Ag cruin-
diughadh dona poibleachaibh ní fhēdaid na foirfidhe én-ní
do dhēnamh a bfēgmhuis na minisdreadh.

Agas dā rabh uireasbhuidh ar āireamh / nó ar uibhir na			[C8v]
bfoirfidheach dlighidh an minisdir, maille rē rēsún agas ré			755
toil chāich, sin do nochtadh don phobal, agas togha mhaith
do dhēnamh ar na daoinibh bhus imchubhaidh do chur ina
n-āit mar orduighthear na minisdrighe do thogha do rēir a
gcáile féin.

agas dā dtogha and so síos.

ghnāth-umhla, agas coinghill mhaithe iondraca do bheith
iondta mar orduidheas an t-easpul naomhtha Pól. Agas así			765
so an oifige agas an obair dhligheas siad do dhēnamh: dēirc
do thinōl agas do thiumsughadh go dītheallach, agas do
roind ar na bo/chtaibh go h-umhal indruic maille ré toil na			[D
Minisdreadh agas na bFoirfidheach, agas freasdal agas
fritheōlamh do dhēnamh ar na daoinibh anbfanda easlāna			770
uireasbhadhacha ēgcruaidhe, ar eagla go gcaithfidhe daon-
dacht na ndaoine maith ré daoinibh dimhaoineacha nō ré
ruagairibh.

[DO] THUIGSIN AN SCRIPTUIR

DO MHINIUGHADH AGAS DO THUIGSIN
an Sgriobhtūir and so síos.

na foghlama nī sa mó iná do bheidīs ar tí throda nō imreasna.

ghean agas ré deagh-chroidhe ar a n-adhaidh do chomh-			2715

gach én-ní. Agas fós, a Thighearna neamhdha, dēna trōcaire
ar gach uile dhuine atá easslán do rēir chuirp nó amhghar eile,		2720
agas fós ar na daoinibh atá a n-ainbfios na fīrinde, an mhéid

DO SMACHTUGHADH NA H-EAGLUISE

aisg dā chēile, agas dīoghaltus agas smachtughadh do
dhēnamh ar na daoinibh dúra droch-mhēine do-theagaisg
do-ní tarcaisne ar an ordughadh-sin.

Atáid trí h-adhbhair sbeisialta do-bheir ar Eagluis Dé an
smachtughadh-sa do chur a ngnīomh: an céd-adhbhar			2775
dhīobh, do chum nach áirfidhe lucht droch-ghnīomharthadh
a measg chloinde Dé, do thabhairt náire nó sgandaile dā
n-Athair, mar go mbeith Eagluis Dé ina dīdean agas ina
tearmond do lucht uilc agas aingidheachta; an dara h-
adhbhar, ar eagla go saileōchaidhe na daoine maithe lé			2780
cumand na ndroch-dhaoine (agas do thairrngir Pól / naomh-			[L7v]
tha sin, an uair tug sé a aithne dona Córuinteachaibh an
t-adhultrach coirpthe colach d’athchur agas d’indarbadh
uathadh, agā rādha mar so: ‘Cuiridh beagān do thaos tsearbh
nó ghoirt an t-arán uile a seirbhe nó a ngoirte’); an treas			2785
adhbhar, do chum go ngēbhadh neach do smachtughadh nó
do chūrsadh náire fāna lochtaibh, agas go dtiucfadh sé mar
sin lé h-aithrighe do chum leighis agas leasaighthe; agas is
don chūrsadh-sa ghoireas an t-Easbul ‘toirbheartas do
tSatān, do chum a anma nō a sbioraide do bheith saor a ló			2790
an Tighearna’; is inand sin agas an droch-dhuine do smach-
tughadh lé cūrsadh ar eagla go rachadh a anam i ndamnadh
suthain.

Mās eadh, is in-chomhtharraidh ar tús go bfuil an smach-
tughadh nó an t-ordughadh-sa nó an riaghail-se, uaigneach			2795
nó follas; uaigneach ar tús, mar atá dā ndēnadh duine locht
chugad do ghnīomharthuibh nó do bhriathruibh, tú dā
chomhairleachadh go brāithreamhail / eadrad féin agas é;			[L8]
agas eisean do dhiūltadh do chomhairle cairdeamhla-sa, nō
fós dá dhearbhadh dhuit lé gnáthughadh a mhí-bhēs nach			2800
bfuil rún ro-leasaighthe aige; and sin, ’na dhiaidh sin an
dara h-uair, a theagasg a bfiadhnuise deise nó trír d’fhiadh-
nuise maith, agas fós eisean d’anmhuin go dúr daingean ina
ainbfios féin agas ina tseachrán, dlighthear and sin, mar
iarras ar Slānuighthōir Crīost, a fhoillseachadh agas a noch-			2805
tadh don Eagluis, iondas, do réir an smachtaighe fhollais,
go dtucthar do chum na h-eagluise lé h-aithrighe é, nó go
ndēntar dīoghaltus do réir a locht air.

[TD 115]

<eng>NOTES

1 <gai>Foirm na n-urrnuidheadh:<eng> M’L translates “forms of prayer”, but foirm is singular and corresponds to the English title ‘The Form of Prayers’. In urrnuidhe spellings with -dh- predominate, though -gh- is found occasionally; the alternation is widespread in this text (e.g. <gai>ina ndiaigh<eng> 38, <gai>a n-adhaidh<eng> 54, <gai>ragha<eng> 59, <gai>ladh<eng> 64, <gai>laodh<eng> 78, <gai>loidhed<eng> 155, <gai>an-arrsaigh<eng> 176) as the two had been identical in sound since the thirteenth century; cf. ID 65. For the -rr- compare <gai>oirrdheirc<eng> 34, <gai>tarrla<eng> 89, <gai>foirrneart<eng> 131, <gai>oirrdhearcas<eng> 137, <gai>tighearrntuis<eng> 147, <gai>fairrge<eng> 170, <gai>an-arrsaigh<eng> 176, and many other examples. Cf. E. Knott’s edition of Tadhg Dall O Huiginn, xcvii, which describes “r medial usually doubled before ch, dh, gh, l, n, s, th, when the preceding vowel is historically short” as the standard of good seventeenth-century manuscripts.

9 <gai>tar ēis an fhuar-chreidimh do chur ar gcūl:<eng> “after abandoning the false faith”. With this use of <gai>fuar<eng> cf. <gai>fuar-chrādhbhadh<eng> 184. Note the construction with gen. after <gai>tar ēis;<eng> similarly, <gai>do chum an t-saothair-sin do dhul a dtarbha<eng> 36, and 247, <gai>a dtimcheall dhroch-chreidimh do sgris<eng> 61, <gai>tar ēis an rīgh agas na n-Eigifteach uile do bhāthadh<eng> 172, <gai>do chum ghlōire agas onōra do thabhairt do Dhia<eng> 443, <gai>tar ēis an chūraim-sin do ghabhāil orra<eng> 685, and so frequently. For the undeclined noun in this position cf. <gai>do chum a fhios do bheith againd<eng> 1764, and Des. p. 266.

10 <gai>arna dtarraing as Laidin:<eng> for the sense “translate” see CDIL svv. tarraing 84.70, do-srenga 377.71. On the language and history of the text that Carswell was translating see the Introduction.

16 <gai>do tsuidhiughadh:<eng> for Carswell’s use of ts for lenited s see the Introduction.

17 1 Cor., 3 11, in the Irish New Testament reads ní héidir ré haoinneach fondameint ar bith eile do chur, taobh amuigh don fhondameint atá air na chur cheana, éadhon Iosa Críosd.

20 For Lekprevik see the Edinburgh Bibliographical Society, vol. VII 116; his activity extended from 1561 to 1582. For the bibliography of BCO see ibid., vol. x 53ff., where William Cowan


[TD 116]

deals with the history and contents as well as enumerating the editions. Lekprevik was the Kirk’s approved printer and we find the General Assembly (BUK 164) assigning him yearly £50 “having respect to his povertie, the great expenses he hes made in buying of printing yrnes, and the great zeale and love he beares to serve the Kirk at all tymes.”

A2 Although displayed by the printer to fill a whole page these lines 22-33 are not strictly a dedication but simply the superscription of the epistle dedicatory. We may compare Archbishop Daniel’s opening in the Irish Prayer Book of 1609: “To the right honorable, and truely religious, Sir Arthur Chichester Knight, L. Deputy generall of Ireland, W. D. wisheth all grace, prosperity and true comfort in this life; and eternall happinesse in the life to come.” The actual words of dedication, as with Carswell 48-50, 265-279, come in the letter itself.

23 <gai>Giolla Easbuig:<eng> Archibald, the fifth earl, born c. 1530, died 1575, further identified as Donn. Cf. Highland Papers, vol. II 101, and cf. ibid. 72 for “ane accompt of the Genealogie of the Campbells who were of old called Clann oduibn (or rather oduibhn) … from the famous Knights and champions the oduibhns, and especially from Diarmad (or Jeremie) oduibhnes famous in the Irish genealogies; from whom they are sometymes designated Siol-Diarmid or Sliochd Diarmad, … but assumed the surname of Campbells in the days of Malcolm the third … who nevertheless do in their own language keep the names both of Campbells and oduibhns to this day.” Hence Ua nDuibhne 24, 402.

24 <gai>Iarrla:<eng> earl at this time, marquess from 1641; the ducal title dates from 1701.

25 <gai>Ard-ghiúisdis<eng> “Justiciary” and <gai>Aird-cheand teaghluigh<eng> “Master of the Household” were titles also held by his father Archibald the fourth earl, who died in 1558.

26 <gai>Lieutenanda:<eng> printed Liue-; -eu- may have been accidentally transposed but -ue-, i.e. -ve-, is also possible. Something of the kind lies behind our present pronunciation of lieutenant, and f or v spellings were current in both Scotland and England in the fifteenth and sixteenth centuries. Cf. Bruce (ms. of 1489) XIV 140 luftenand, and NED s.v. 1c, quotations of 1596, leauetennant (Scots), Lievetenant (English). With this preservation of v instead of substituting bh, cf. <gai>reuerens,<eng> -ach, in CC. The appointment of the earls of Argyll


[TD 117]

as royal lieutenants (the title was then used with its etymological sense of immediate deputy) dates from 1517 (Gregory, p. 119), and seems to have become hereditary.

29 <gai>ag guidhe:<eng> the consonant of ag is regularly assimilated to a following c- or g- and so is omitted in Carswell’s spelling. Its loss before other consonants is not yet recognised by the spelling.

33 <gai>ō Dhia Athar:<eng> gen. of apposition; cf. Des. p. 246, and below 303, 1115, 1146.

35 <gai>ar buil:<eng> “in force”, “practised”, cf. CC 336 a 3; for the nom. bail see TSh 5118.

36 <gai>dul a dtarbha do:<eng> “profit, benefit”; see CDIL s.v. torbae 258.7, and cf. below 693.

41 <gai>airidhe:<eng> also 46, 55. See SGS II 13-14. The <gai>d’<eng> represents the preposition <gai>do,<eng> and marks the predicative status of the noun, e.g. 41 “and who is best as one worthy of it”, “who is most worthy of it”. For predicative <gai>do<eng> cf. <gai>d’fhiachaibh<eng> 92, <gai>do thidhlocadh<eng> 241, <gai>d’aon-iodhbairt<eng> 482, <gai>dh’athair<eng> 1516, 1727.

41 <gai>is mó ar a mbī cumaoin ghrāidh fhir dhēnta an t-saothair:<eng> lit. “and upon whom is the greatest recompense for the love of the author of the work”, i.e. those who by virtue of their affection for the author have most deserved this gift of the dedication of his book. Similarly 47, <gai>méd do chomaoine grāidh imarcaidh oram,<eng> lit. “the extent of your entitlement of abundant love toward me”, i.e. “the extent of the obligation I owe you for your great love toward me.”

44 <gai>agā thuigsin:<eng> “understanding it”, where the pronoun anticipates the various objects to follow. In such cases it is always masculine singular, even when, as here, the first object is feminine and the objects in the aggregate are plural. Cf. <gai>agā rāgha<eng> 58, <gai>agā fhoillsiughadh<eng> 580, <gai>agā admhāil agas ’gā thuigsin<eng> 830, <gai>agā dhearbhadh<eng> 868, 898, and similar cases like <gai>a aithne do thabhairt dúinn gach aon againd do dhēnamh guidhe<eng> 1583, 2920.

46 <gai>th’fheabhus:<eng> before an initial vowel or lenited f- the possessive of the 2nd. pers. sg. regularly has the form h- in Carswell for the etymological th’ (which occurs before vowels at 1523, 3037, 3068, and is restored in this edition). The f-, as is also usual after the preposition d’, is not marked as lenited.

48 <gai>do condcus damh-sa:<eng> impersonal preterite “has been seen” in the sense of “has seemed (good) to me”. The sense “was seen” is


[TD 118]

found at 1945. Similarly <gai>do-cīthear<eng> “seems good” 1125, cf. 1296. At 2886 the active is used to render “seem”.

48 <gai>gurbh in-dēnta damh … do thoirbheart:<eng> “that I must present”. The use of the in- prefix to form a new verbal of necessity is not nearly so common here as in CC, and some cases of the older type without the prefix are also found, as <gai>tuicthe<eng> 218, <gai>gabhtha<eng> 228, <gai>creite<eng> 502, <gai>beite<eng> 1746. On the use of <gai>dēnamh<eng> as an auxiliary see the Introduction.

55 <gai>do dhul ar neifnī:<eng> “perish”, lit. “go into nothing”; <gai>neif-<eng> is a rarer form of the negative prefix <gai>neamh-, neimh-,<eng> as in Old Irish nephní gl. nihil (GOI § 874).

58 <gai>gē tāid:<eng> the pretonic a- of <gai>atá, atáid, atámaoid,<eng> disappears after <gai>gé<eng> “though”, <gai>ó<eng> “since”, and and <gai>má<eng> “if”.

62 <gai>inā:<eng> “than”; this is Carswell’s regular form, for which see CDIL s.v. indaas. He also has two examples of <gai>nō<eng> 669, 1419; see CDIL s.v. 7 nó, “occasionally used in late texts”.

63 <gai>neoch:<eng> relative pronoun, not as a defining but as a descriptive relative, adding further information; cf. 1744.

68 <gai>an toil-sin Dé:<eng> “that will of God”; when the noun upon which a noun in the gen. depends is qualified by a demonstrative the article is used, although there would be no article in <gai>toil Dé<eng> “the will of God”. Cf. <gai>an bás-sin ar Slānaidhthōir<eng> 487, <gai>cló na h-īmhāidhe-sin Iosa Crīosd<eng> 1992, <gai>na briathra-sa Pōil<eng> 2146, <gai>na h-easslāinte-sin agas na teachtairidhe an bháis<eng> 2541 (where the demonstrative is to be understood as applying to teachtairidhe also), <gai>an t-slat-sa na n-easbul<eng> 2848.

71 <gai>sgrībhtha:<eng> predicative, “in written form”.

81 <gai>Dē na n-uile chumhacht:<eng> “of God almighty”, a more idiomatic equivalent of the adjective <gai>uile-chumhachtach,<eng> which nevertheless occurs fairly frequently, e.g. 1115, 1146, 1169, 1174.

86 <gai>Isafat:<eng> Jehoshaphat; <gai>Esecias,<eng> Hezekiah; <gai>Iosias,<eng> Josiah. Carswell uses the forms from the LXX and Vulgate transliterations of the Hebrew names, and accordingly <gai>Isafat<eng> is probably a misprint for <gai>Iosafat.<eng> The printer seems to have had some difficulty with <gai>Esecias,<eng> which appears as <gai>Esasias<eng> here, and <gai>Esisias<eng> at 140. This paragraph recalls the argument set forth in the confession of faith adopted on 17 August 1560 (APS II 534), in the section “Of the civile Magistrat”. “Mairouer to Kingis Princes rewlaris and magistratis we affirme that cheiflie and maist principallie the conversatioun and


[TD 119]

purgatioun of the Religioun appertenis … as in David, Josaphat, Ezechias, Josias, and vtheris heichly commendit for thair zeall in that caiss may be espyit.”

88 <gai>an Sbirad Naoimh:<eng> gen. sg.; for the declension of the noun in Carswell’s usage see the Introduction. The adjective generally remains unchanged but occasionally inflects as here and at 461.

89 <gai>Asē adhbhar fā dtarrla dhāibh bheith:<eng> “this is why they were”, lit. “this is the reason on account of which it befell them to be.” <gai>Asē<eng> is <gai>is é,<eng> with <gai>as<eng> (originally the relative form) for <gai>is;<eng> cf. Bergin’s Stories from Keating’s History of Ireland, iv. Tarrla is a subjectless verb occurring only in the past tense, for which see CDIL s.v. do-cuirethar 233.39, 235.28, 236.75, and especially 237.73. Independently in 207; “become” in 1842. The verbal noun <gai>beith<eng> is regularly lenited, even without preceding <gai>do,<eng> and the example to the contrary at 63 should probably be corrected; cf. Des. p. 270.

91 <gai>nō go raibhe sē d’fhiachaibh orra:<eng> “or that it was as a debt upon them”, i.e. “that they were under an obligation to do so.” <gai>Nō go<eng> is frequently “until” as at 1143, but here the elements are to be construed separately.

95 <gai>an phobaill gheindtlidhe:<eng> cf. 115. The plural would seem more appropriate, and indeed Carswell often uses it even when referring to a single group, as <gai>ag buaidhreadh na bpoibleach<eng> 213, and cf. 345, 666, 689, 805.

97 <gai>do dhlighfedīs:<eng> conditional with pluperfect sense, “which they ought to have given.”

115 <gai>ag an phoball … go bfuillid:<eng> the -ll in <gai>pobal<eng> is paralleled at 95, 1186, but that in go bfuillid is not, and is probably a misprint.

119 <gai>daona:<eng> similarly 1562; better <gai>daonda<eng> 204, <gai>daonna<eng> 991. Cf. CDIL s.v. doénda.

120 <gai>gach uile nī rigmaoid a leas:<eng> “everything we need”; for the idiom cf. CDIL s.v. ro-icc 91.70. The a is the possessive “its”, but at 1419 and 2516 Carswell has <gai>do leas<eng> as if he took a as a weakened form of do, as he sometimes has a for do with a verbal noun, as at 98, 153, 214, 708.

127 <gai>dā gach neach:<eng> “to everyone”; before gach the preposition regularly has the form dā not <gai>do,<eng> as at 604, 803, 1463. Cf. CDIL s.v. 1 do 171.21.

148 <gai>ar comhairle tSatāin, .i. an ainsbiraid, nāmha …:<eng> “by the


[TD 120]

counsel of Satan, i.e. the evil spirit, the enemy.” Cf. note 88. This is the only example of the nom. sg. fem. type.

149 <gai>dhā mhēd:<eng> “however great”; for the construction and following <gai>dá,<eng> see Des. p. 253, and for further examples 320, and <gai>dā fhad<eng> 3500.

151 <gai>cuntabhairt:<eng> in the later sense of “danger”, as at 183 (paired with <gai>gábhadh<eng> and <gai>guasacht<eng>), not in the older sense of “doubt” as at 202, 640. Modern forms are Sc.G. <gai>cunnart,<eng> Irish contúirt.

153 <gai>agas ard-mhonadh … ar gach taobh dībh, agas Faró … ’na ndiaidh:<eng> the verbless “and” clauses, primarily descriptive, are here virtually concessive, “although there was.” Cf. CDIL s.v. 2 ocus (d).

160 <gai>gan dul:<eng> “not going”; here consecutive, “so that he did not go.”

161 <gai>uathmhur:<eng> so here and at 846, 1542, but subsequently the form <gai>fuathmhur<eng> with prosthetic f- is used, e.g. 1899.

165 <gai>a h-ucht an Sbirad Naoimh:<eng> similarly <gai>a h-ucht agas a n-ainm a Mhic<eng> 3628; cf. for the first example CDIL s.v. ucht 53.48 (f) “by the influence of” (parallel to <gai>ar aithne Dhé<eng>), and 53.1 (b) “in the name of” for the second.

178 <gai>as a chrand-tabhaill:<eng> see CDIL s.v. táball.

181 <gai>dār thinnsgain tū … dā raibhe oraibh:<eng> the generalising relative dá (dár with the preterite tense) after an antecedent qualified by <gai>gach;<eng> in the second case the antecedent is not explicitly qualified but the sense is “whatever danger”. Note the singular pronoun tú and the plural oraibh referring to the same person in the same sentence. The use of the pl. is a mark of respect but Carswell does not use it at all consistently; cf. Des. p. 251. On the other hand he intermittently uses the first pl. of himself, e.g. <gai>do rāidheamar romhaind<eng> 180, but <gai>ní fuil a n-ainbfis damh-sa<eng> 195, where the pl. would not have the same meaning. Cf. also <gai>dhaoibh-se … dhuit-se<eng> 217-8, <gai>ar nGaoidhealg … mo dheagh-thoil uaim-se<eng> 228-9, <gai>ribh … dhuit<eng> 265-8.

186 <gai>a ndēntaoi … do bhunadh:<eng> Professor Matheson understood this as “in which false sacrifices were accustomed to be established”, though there seems to be no other evidence for <gai>bunadh<eng> as a verbal noun. The phrase esbaid do bunad in CIL, rendered “loss outright”, suggests that perhaps do bhunadh should be taken with <gai>leagadh agas lān-bhriseadh,<eng> i.e. “the utter overthrowing and smash-


[TD 121]

ing of altars and places where false sacrifices were performed,” or, with <gai>dēntaoi,<eng> “were performed from of old.”

191 <gai>mar atā:<eng> introducing examples or instances, “such as”; there is a general distinction in Carswell between <gai>mar atá<eng> “namely” (more rarely the pl. <gai>mar atáid<eng> 2856), and <gai>mar tá sé<eng> “as it is”, e.g. 312, 362, but <gai>mar tá<eng> is occasionally found in this sense, as 604, 1107. Cf. CDIL s.v. immar II (a) 125.79.

203 <gai>go bfuilit-seán:<eng> -it for the usual -id because of the voiceless sound following, cf. CDIL s.v. sin 231.48; -seán for -séan, with the length-mark misplaced as often; cf. -séan 552, 1748, -sean 379, 1728, -soin 1148, -sion 1179, -sun 393, and CDIL s.v. 1 sin 231.22, .33.

210 <gai>na h-Iūbhail:<eng> “the Jews”; but in <gai>a gcrīch na n-Iodhbhal<eng> 465 “in the land of the Jews” (like <gai>a gcrīochaibh Earra-Gaoidheal<eng> 13 “in Argyll”) we have what is probably a different form, as is suggested by <gai>lucht cumhdaigh … na n-īodhbhal<eng> 618 “idolaters”. Cf. CDIL s.vv. Ibal, and ídal senses (a) and (c).

215 <gai>dhā iarraidh:<eng> the equivalent of <gai>agá iarraidh<eng> and parallel to <gai>ag nochtadh;<eng> cf. Bergin’s Stories from Keating’s History of Ireland, p. 81, and CDIL s.v. 1 do 172.55. Similarly <gai>dā chrīochnughadh<eng> 320 “being completed”, <gai>atā siad do dheilbh<eng> 1612, <gai>neoch ata tú anois do smachtughadh<eng> 2576.

220 <gai>ar son chothaighe agas fhuatha:<eng> gen. of the verbal noun <gai>cothughadh,<eng> more correctly spelt <gai>cothaighthe;<eng> the usual senses are “maintain, support” but CIL adds also “contend, strive”, meanings which seem more appropriate to <gai>cathughadh,<eng> which Carswell also uses. The examples at 381, 1338, 1380, 1389, 2194 all belong to the general notion of fighting, resisting, rebelling Cf. <gai>cothughadh maith do dhēnamh a n-adhaidh ar nāmhad<eng> 1707, and <gai>fā chathughadh cruaidh do dhēnamh a n-adhaidh na nāmhad-sin<eng> 1718. But at 2949 <gai>do chothughadh agas do sheasamh th’ adhbhuir<eng> is ‘to maintain thy cause’.

229 <gai>mh’uireasbhadha: mo<eng> loses its vowel before a word beginning in a vowel and is lenited, cf. 272. Similarly with <gai>do,<eng> on which see note 46. Cf. Des. p. 360, and CDIL s.vv. mo 151.84, and 2 do 176.70.

230 <gai>oram:<eng> Carswell has only the disyllabic form while Keating and Des. have both; cf. DIL s.v. for 294.44.

230 <gai>a gcanamhain Ghaoidheilge:<eng> the phrase means simply “in


[TD 122]

Gaelic”, as does <gai>a gcanamhain Ghaoidhilge<eng> 353, and as <gai>ar gcanamhna Gaoidheilge<eng> 307 means “our Gaelic”, and as <gai>ceart canamhna na Gaoidheilge<eng> 366 is equivalent to “correct Gaelic”. The Metrical Psalms, both 1659 and 1694, have <gai>a Neaglaisaibh agus a Dteaghlachaibh a ghnathuigheas an chanamhain sin<eng> “in congregations and households that use that language”, but note that later in his catechism Carswell uses <gai>cánoin, canóin.<eng>

232 <gai>don tí is eōlcha iná mé:<eng> “to the one who is better informed than I”. <gai>In tí<eng> represents the article <gai>in t-<eng> with the demonstrative <gai>í,<eng> later analysed as containing a noun <gai>tí<eng> “person”, or as a pronoun “the one”; under the influence of the pronoun <gai>sé, é,<eng> a secondary form <gai>té<eng> developed as at 673, 1011, which in Irish refers, like an tí, to masculine or both genders, but in Sc.G. to the feminine.

237 <gai>acht gé:<eng> simply “although” or “even though”; <gai>acht,<eng> as often, has no separate adversative force. Cf. 315, 380, <gai>acht mura<eng> 363, <gai>acht mara<eng> 368 “unless”, <gai>acht mun<eng> 1672, <gai>acht mur<eng> 2700.

240 <gai>gē bé dā dtug Dia do thidhlocadh bheith eōlach:<eng> lit. “though there be to whom God has given as a gift to be learned”, i.e. “whoever God has given the gift of being learned”; <gai>bé<eng> is pres. subj. of <gai>beith.<eng> For predicative <gai>do<eng> see note 41.

241 <gai>a gceart chanamhna … na Gaoidheilge:<eng> cf. the similar phrases cited in note 230. That at 366 is a close parallel to the text here, and shows that it can be interpreted as “expert in the correct form of the language of writing and composing and speaking Gaelic”, with <gai>sgrībhadh<eng> and <gai>deachtadh<eng> treated as synonymous. The other examples give some support to an emended reading <gai>a gceart sgrībhaidh agas deachtaidh agas labhartha chanamhna na Gaoidheilge,<eng> i.e. “in the correct form of writing, composing, and speaking the Gaelic language”, with <gai>sgrībhadh<eng> and <gai>deachtadh<eng> differentiated to denote orthography and style respectively.

247 <gai>mhé:<eng> and also 254, 384, in each case as object, as is the solitary example in Des. 800, cf. p. 360. For unlenited mé as subject see 380, 381, 383, 388.

251 <gai>lēm bhrāithribh:<eng> I have doubtfully corrected to show the lenition, but its absence can be justified (cf. Des. xxiii), and according to Bergin, IGT iii, is the only correct usage. However, the rule given at IGT Introduction § 52 Lom as cóir do gháoidheilg íad 7 séimh d’ogham “they ought to be pronounced unlenited and written lenited”, may be cited in extenuation.


[TD 123]

257 <gai>gurab oide … thú … gurab cádhas … thú … gurab athair … thú:<eng> the pronoun <gai>tú<eng> is regularly found lenited in independent use, as 411, 3298, and as object 880, while tú is the usual form as the subject pronoun following a verb. Similarly with the emphatic <gai>tusa.<eng> The lenition may be inhibited under the usual circumstances of another dental preceding, as <gai>tar éis tusa d’fhosgladh dhorais dúinn<eng> 902.

258 <gai>cádhas:<eng> already at 101 (paired with <gai>onóir<eng>) and 114 (paired with <gai>cúrum<eng> and <gai>creideamh<eng>), with the usual sense of “respect, reverence”; here paired with <gai>comairce<eng> and having the same sense as in CC, i.e. “refuge, protection”. Similarly below, 3008, it is paired with <gai>tearmand.<eng>

262 <gai>timpiridhe:<eng> pl. of <gai>timpire;<eng> cf. CDIL s.v. timthire “servant”, and for -mp- ibid. 176.4, .9, .31, .44-5, .56.

270 <gai>ris na h-ūdaraibh:<eng> “author”, and also “an authority” on some subject; in the latter sense here, paired with <gai>saoi.<eng> A more etymological spelling <gai>údhdar<eng> (for <gai>úghdar<eng>) at 3493.

278 <gai>acht go mbeith:<eng> “provided that there was”; cf. <gai>acht mar<eng> 362 “except as, except in the way that”.

282 <gai>Dia … do choimhēd:<eng> “may God preserve”; neither CDIL, s.v. 1 do 172.25 nor Gagnepain, SNV, treats this construction independently or fully. It occurs early, in St. Patrick’s Hymn, Thes. II 354-8 (an eight-century composition, cf. Binchy in Eriu XX 235), as 357.10 Crist domm imdegail indiu ar neim ar loscud ar bádud ar guin translated “Christ to protect me today …”, but in Mael Isu’s hymn (ibid. 359) In spirut nóeb d’aittreb ar cuirp is ar nanma diar snádud co solma ar gábud ar galra the translation is “May the Holy Spirit dwell … may He protect us …”. That the usage is not necessarily a literary or artificial one in Carswell is suggested by its frequent occurrence in the Manx Prayer Book of 1610, e.g. p. 364 (a form of absolution) and Psalm 20 vv. 1-5, but not in the later language. Further examples below at 390-4, 1163-8, and especially in the prayer of his own composition 1402-13.

290 <gai>Bīdh amhlaidh:<eng> “so be it”, for <gai>bīodh.<eng> The foreign “amen” is generally translated in BCO, and Carswell has amen only at 3803, 3965.

296 <gai>do chum an lēghthōir:<eng> Carswell prefers the uninflected gen. sg. here and in <gai>Slánuightheóir,<eng> and this is authorised by IGT Declension § 50 for léghthóir beside the gen. in -óra and that in


[TD 124]

-órach. Keating prefers the gen. in -óra, as in TSh 7985, and the Metrical Psalms have do chum an leghthora, as well as the more exceptional use of this form as a voc. sg., cf. CC xxxvi.

299 <gai>d’fhearaibh Alban agas Eireand:<eng> on the relevance of Eireand here and below see Introduction.

300 <gai>briathra dísle Dé:<eng> “the genuine Word of God”; Carswell regularly uses the pl. in this sense, e.g. 558, 560, 563, 568.

305 <gai>acht atā nī cheana:<eng> an introductory formula, prefacing a point to be made, cf. 350; also <gai>acht cheana<eng> 341, 361, 375.

306 <gai>atā riamh orainde:<eng> “which has always been upon us” (and still is, whence the present tense), i.e. “from which we have always suffered”.

311 <gai>gan an Bīobla Naomhtha do bheith a gcló Gaoidheilge againd:<eng> “that we do not possess the Holy Bible printed in Gaelic”; the New Testament appeared in Irish in 1602, and the Old in 1685, but Gaelic Scotland had to wait until 1767 for the New Testament, and until 1801 for the whole of the Old, partly because of the belief that the Irish version was quite suitable for use in the Highlands, the language of which was usually termed Irish or Erse. For the gen. <gai>Gaoidheilge,<eng> cf. <gai>a gcló Laidne<eng> 312.

315 <gai>seanchus ar sean nō ar sindsear:<eng> cf. 1316, and sompladha a sean 7 a sinnsear translating exempla parentum in TSh 5335.

317 <gai>a dtāmhlorgaibh:<eng> cf. Acallamh (Irische Texte IV i) 300, scríbhthar na scéla sin letsa i támhlorguibh filed 7 i mbriathraibh ollaman, 2589 … i tamlorgaib filed 7 a slechtaib suad 7 a mbriathraib ollaman, 3105 … i tamlorgaib filed 7 il-lechtaib fiann; these examples suggest a standard formula which need have conveyed no very precise impression to the hearer. Stokes, ibid. 274, explains as “tablestave”, a contraction of tabul-lorg, whence CDIL taball.

317 <gai>a sleachtaibh:<eng> for the sense “version (of a story)”, passing over into the sense of “source” see CDIL s.v. slicht (b), and Acallamh 2589 quoted above.

319 <gai>ar aibrisge agas ar aithghiorra:<eng> the usual meaning of <gai>aibrisge<eng> is “brittleness, frailty” (so CDIL s.v. aiprisce and Vendryes’ Lexique s.v. aprisc) but the synonymous <gai>aithghiorra<eng> shows that the sense here must be “quickness’. A similar sense is possible in Betha Colaim Chille (ed. O’Kelleher and Schoepperle) p. 120.34 do bi se aibrisc dochum aighte do tabhairt d’aes eladhna 7 d’filedhaib; the editors translate “he was weak in indulging bards and poets”, but


[TD 125]

it might also be rendered “he had a weakness for giving countenance to”, and it is easy to see how in this context this might be taken as “he was quick to show favour”. The word is not found in any of the Sc.G. dictionaries, but the simplex <gai>brisg,<eng> which in earlier Irish had the same senses as <gai>aibrisg,<eng> has in Sc.G. developed the sense of “quick”, and similarly the derivative <gai>brisge.<eng>

324 <gai>ghnāthuidheas siad:<eng> with which goes <gai>do chumhdach<eng> 329.

326 <gai>ar Thuathaibh Dé Danond, agas ar Mhacaibh Mīleadh, agas ar na curadhaibh, agas ar Fhind mhac Cumhaill:<eng> interesting testimony that in the sixteenth century stories of the mythological cycle, of the fictitious history of the settlement of Ireland, of the Finn cycle, and unspecified heroic tales, perhaps of the Ulster cycle, were circulating and giving pleasure in Gaelic Scotland, and that patrons were still supporting those who could recite them, as well as giving generously to be themselves immortalised in verse. It is one of the less happy aspects of originally alien religious movements like the Reformation and the Methodist revival that in Celtic territory they did so much to discourage the continuance of native tradition and oral literature. There was a lively if not always well-informed correspondence on the Kirk and Gaelic in the columns of “The Scotsman” in January and February 1966.

336 <gai>dār n-ainbfios … an drong-sa …:<eng> we might expect <gai>dā n-ainbfios,<eng> i.e. “of the ignorance of these we have mentioned”, but Carswell includes himself and his readers in the number of those whose ignorance stems from lack of instruction and good books in their own language. Similarly <gai>oraind<eng> 338.

349 <gai>do bhí:<eng> the past tense refers to the original book having been drawn up for the English-speaking congregation in exile at Geneva. The progress of the Reformation, both in England and Scotland, had now enabled them to return home.

354 <gai>nī rachaind féin a seilbh:<eng> “I should not myself have undertaken”; for <gai>dul a seilbh<eng> see CDIL s.v. selb 164.7.

362 <gai>an Gaill-bhērla:<eng> here, as in 10, with the original meaning of <gai>Bērla<eng> as a common noun (OIr bélre) “language”, and so “the Lowlanders’ tongue, English”. For other meanings see CIL 204.

367 <gai>nī h-a n-Albain amhāin:<eng> “not only in Scotland”; printed here as <gai>ni na,<eng> as on several occasions below, e.g. 900, 1788, 1807. That h- follows ní before even an unstressed vowel is shown by <gai>nī h-ar son mhaithis<eng> 860. The passage is interesting as showing that


[TD 126]

Carswell recognised Scottish Gaelic, in its literary form at least, as the same as the literary dialect of Irish.

369 <gai>do mhacaibh maithe lēighind:<eng> but <gai>mac léighind<eng> normally refers to a pupil or student (cf. CDIL s.v. léigenn 84.46), and we might expect <gai>d’fhearaibh<eng> here unless Carswell was misled by some such ambiguity as surrounds English “scholar”.

374 <gai>mar gach nduine:<eng> for the nasalisation cf. <gai>ar gach nduine<eng> 1001, <gai>dā gach nduine<eng> 3288.

398 <gai>adhmad:<eng> cf. CDIL s.v. admat (b), and Celtica IV 46. The modern Irish sense of “material, wood”, and the Manx ymmyd “use”, are later developments.

404 <gai>dó soirbhidh sén:<eng> impv. 2 sg., better <gai>soirbhigh;<eng> “speed good fortune (or, blessing) to him.”

434 <gai>nach bfētar:<eng> impersonal present of <gai>fédaim<eng> “I can”; the spelling with -t-, which is regular with Carswell, represents what might more etymologically be written fédtar. Cf. DIL s.v. fétaid 100.46.

451 <gai>as go mbeith sé:<eng> “so that it might be”; as is apparently a clipped form of <gai>iondas,<eng> frequent in this text, as 684, 1322, 2526, 2603, 2633. Perhaps we should compare modern Sc.G <gai>os gum bi<eng> “in order that there will be”, or <gai>’s<eng> in the conjunctions <gai>a chum ’s gu, eagal ’s gu, ionann ’s gu<eng> (Calder, Gaelic Grammar § 222).

454 <gai>frīth;<eng> preterite impersonal “one found”; cf. DIL s.v. fo-gaib 226.31.

457 <gai>acht Iosa … cland dhīleas do Dhia dhīnde:<eng> ‘but only Jesus Christ our Lord, who, giving us that by grace, which was his by nature, made us through faith the children of God.’ The words ‘giving us that by grace’ and ‘through faith’ are not translated, so that ‘which was his by nature’ is left without an antecedent. The phrase <eng>d’fhulang … ndīmhaoinis-ne<eng> is Carswell’s own addition, “except that the gracious Jesus of Nazareth should suffer passion and pain for our worthlessness”, but does not complete the sense, and a line seems to have dropped out at some stage. We might insert after <gai>nādúir<eng> something like <gai>do thabhairt dhúinn lé grás, iondas gurab tré chreideamh do-rinde …,<eng> making the Gaelic mean “that he should give us by grace that which was his by nature, so that through faith he has made us true children of God.”

470 <gai>nīrbh ēidir:<eng> preterite of the copula with the predicative <gai>éidir,<eng> and so generally in Carswell, but occasionally spellings with


[TD 127]

<gai>féidir,<eng> arising either from the common prosthetic f- or a supposed connection with <gai>fédaim,<eng> occur as at 471, where <gai>nīr<eng> is very improbably made to nasalise such a spelling to <gai>bfēidir.<eng> As these are the only cases in which bf occurs for etymological bh it is evident that they arise from a false analysis of the source of a sound which could originate equally in the lenition of b and the nasalisation of f. Other instances are 1866, 1914, 2255, and <gai>nīor bfada<eng> 3240.

479 <gai>crēd tug don thrēigean tú?:<eng> “what has caused thee to forsake me?”; the Irish PB version (Mark 15. 34, f. T2 verso) has créd far thréig tú mhé.

490 <gai>ar an treas tráth:<eng> for the meaning “day” cf. CDIL s.v. tráth I (b).

493 <gai>an da choda … na coda eile:<eng> ‘and therefore without the one we cannot feel the benefit of the other’; accordingly read <gai>an dara coda.<eng> For an here cf. CC 234 a 2 <gai>a dtimchiol an dara codach,<eng> and 221 a 1 <gai>seadh an dara cuid.<eng>

495 <gai>fuaramair-ne:<eng> the 1 pl. pret. ends in -amar, but the palatal -ne of the augens sometimes affects the otherwise non-palatal final of the word to which it is suffixed. Cf. <gai>ar ar soin-ne<eng> 504, <gai>mēdaidh ar gcreidimh-ne<eng> 1052, <gai>iondas go bfédaim-ne<eng> 3101.

505 <gai>ar chuir:<eng> ar represents the relative preposition <gai>a<eng> (nas.) “in which” with <gai>ro,<eng> and occurs again at 1838; the usual form in Carswell is <gai>inar<eng> as at 205, 2254, 2946, <gai>’nar<eng> 716.

515 <gai>do ridhise:<eng> occurs only here for Carswell’s usual <gai>a rís;<eng> for the etymology, which justifies this spelling, see DIL s.v. frithissi 442.41.

530 <gai>ēraic:<eng> originally a payment, specialised in Irish law as a term for legal compensation for injury or death, here used in a rather different and theological sense of ransom or the price paid to redeem.

564 <gai>do chomhall:<eng> only here and at 629 <gai>gan a comhall;<eng> the sense appears to be “fulfil, perform”. Cf. CIL s.v. comall, and TSh 6138.

565 <gai>Agas adubhairt an Tighearna féin:<eng> added by Carswell.

574 <gai>mar signuidhfear sin:<eng> ‘as it is signified’, so that there is no call here for a future, and even if there were we should expect <gai>signeóchar<eng> (or cf. CC 81 a 1 shigneobhas). The normal spelling would be <gai>mar shignighthear,<eng> with the usual lenition after mar; the -f-, if not simply a misprint, may be an echo of signify.


[TD 128]

575 <gai>eidir óg agas tsean:<eng> “both young and old”, with the lenition carried forward after <gai>agas,<eng> as in <gai>edir anam agas chorp<eng> 3929. The case usage agrees with Des. p. 271, i.e. singular noun in the accusative, as 70, 466, 1897, and plural noun in the dative as 626, 780, 817; and also, when a second noun follows after <gai>agas<eng> it is nominative after the article or a possessive, otherwise dative, as <gai>eidir chorp agas anmain<eng> 646, and cf. 1953-4.

614 <gai>ag tarcaisne:<eng> for <gai>tarcaisne<eng> as a quasi verbal noun cf. CDIL s.v. tarcaisne 78.70; similarly at 909 with <gai>dímheas,<eng> though both are nouns at 896.

618 <gai>lucht cumhdaigh agas comh-onōraigh na n-īodhbhal neamh-chosmhail:<eng> ‘against all idolaters’; <gai>īodhbhal<eng> seems to be a blend of the two words referred to in note 210. That there was some overlap between the two terms is apparent from CDIL s.v. ídal (c). <gai>Neamh-chosmhail<eng> occurs only here and at 798; there it means “eccentric, abnormal”, here perhaps simply “strange”.

620 <gai>do tharraing as a bfrēmhaibh:<eng> not, as in the different construction of the English, “to root out”, but “who have rooted out”.

624 Between the mention of the mass and prayer to saints Carswell omits the references to purgatory and limbus patrum, and between prayers for the dead and distinction of meats (626) he omits the reference to free-will. After <gai>seirbhīs īodhalta<eng> (629) he has nothing to correspond to ‘man’s merits, with suchlike, which draw us from the society of Christ’s Church, wherein standeth only remission of sins, purchased by Christ’s blood to all them that believe, whether they be Jews or Gentiles’, and proceeds straight to ‘vain confidence in creatures and trust in our own imaginations.’

631 <gai>do-bheir Dia cairde agas síneadh:<eng> ‘God … deferreth’; for the latter word cf. CC 371 b 2 dlighidh sé síneadh do chur jonta.

635 <gai>daingnithear … lucht na n-olc-sa:<eng> the usual meanings of the verb are “confirm, strengthen, assure”, but the English has ‘they shall be damned’, and a future is called for after <gai>an uair thiucfas<eng> 633. The choice lies between the f-future damainfithear (DIL D 66.58) and, less probably, the e-future daimeóntar. Professor Thomson suggests that though the spelling is assimilated to that of <gai>daingniughadh<eng> the true source is the loanword that appears in modern Lewis Gaelic in <gai>daing ort<eng> “a curse on you”, and <gai>daingit<eng> “dammit”, both with non-Gaelic d, and to be connected with English dang as a form of damn. It is unfortunately impossible to


[TD 129]

confirm this as there is no evidence in NED or either of the Scottish historical dictionaries for dang as early as this, but this is neither unexpected nor a serious objection.

641 <gai>Tigidh:<eng> similarly <gai>gabhaidh<eng> 642, <gai>sloindidh<eng> 1766. There is no trace here or in CC of the Sc.G. imperative in -ibh, though it seems certain that it existed before Carswell’s time in some areas at least; cf. Scottish Verse from the Book of the Dean of Lismore, xxiv.

644 <gai>go ngairdeachas n-adhbhal:<eng> the only example of <gai>go<eng> (nas.), preposition with the acc. meaning “with”, in this text, though examples of <gai>gona<eng> “with its” are more frequent. Note the nasalisation after the acc. sg. projected on both adjectives.

647 <gai>mar a bfaicfeam:<eng> “where we shall see”; cf. CDIL s.v. immar II (e).

648 <gai>gnúis fri gnúis:<eng> the use of <gai>fri<eng> in these three phrases is a conscious archaism for the normal <gai>ré;<eng> cf. DIL s.v. fri 413.79.

653 <gai>tré bhioth sīor:<eng> “for ever”, from <gai>bioth<eng> “world, life”, as in <gai>ar bioth<eng> 1820. Cf. Des. glossary s.v. bioth for evidence of both <gai>síor<eng> (acc.) and <gai>shíor<eng> (dat.) in this phrase.

654 <gai>oifige:<eng> so here and at 741, 760 (dat.), and at 661, 664, 686 (nom.). Though not paralleled elsewhere these examples are too numerous to be dismissed as misprints.

654-740 In the section on church government which now follows Carswell has inverted the order of the first two items of the English book, and first states the purpose and powers of the ministry, then turns to the qualifications, and finally deals with the procedure for election. The change is an improvement, but in the process he has removed the heading of the third part ‘The manner of electing the pastors and ministers’ and substituted the introductory sentence 699-700.

658 <gai>diosmasaid:<eng> “dispensation”, apparently a modified form of the loanword recorded in CDIL s.v. dispensáid; the nasal of the second syllable seems to have become -m- by assimilation and the -p- then lost and the nasal become syllabic.

666 <gai>briathra Dé d’fhoillsiughadh:<eng> the English has ‘in preaching the Word of God and ministering the sacraments. So that in consultations, judgments, elections, and other political affairs, his counsel rather than authority taketh place.’ Carswell omits the mention of the sacraments here and alters the division of the rest of the sentence to mean “to proclaim the Word of God in comfortings


[TD 130]

and in judgments and in election and in other worldly affairs; and he ought rather to advise the people than attempt to impose his authority upon them.” He seems to have taken the terms “election” and “judgment” in a more specifically theological sense than the context warrants. Dr W. D. Maxwell seems to go to the opposite extreme when in his Liturgical Portions of the Genevan Service Book, 168, he notes: “this is an interesting statement of the Ministers’ relation to civic and secular politics,” for the “consultations, judgments and elections” all most naturally refer to the affairs of the congregation, its disciplinary jurisdiction and its election of elders and deacons, and “political” is merely the adjective from “policy”, the whole system of organisation of the Church, as in the Book of Discipline “concerning the Polecie of the Churche” (Laing II 237) or CC 171 b 2 do choimhéd Politia, no ordaighthe oirrdherce na heaglaise. In this sense we now use polity instead of policy.

679 <gai>dona coingheallaibh:<eng> cf. <gai>deagh-choinghill agas deigh-bhēsa<eng> 683; the senses appear to be “qualifications” and “good qualities”, a development from the sense of “pledge, guarantee”, through that of “distinguishing mark”.

690 <gai>deithneas:<eng> parallel to <gai>cūram,<eng> and so “eagerness, willingness” rather than “haste”.

701 <gai>féchadh … ar an adhbhar ministir:<eng> “look out for” gives good sense, with “examine, look into” in 708; for the use of <gai>adhbhar<eng> (like <gai>damhna<eng>) as “the makings of, one fit to be” cf. CDIL s.v. adbar I (c), and <gai>adhbhair an mhinistir<eng> 708.

703 <gai>na daoine foirfe:<eng> i.e. the elders; cf. below 741-59. Originally the participle of <gai>for-fen<eng> “finish”, i.e. <gai>foirbthe,<eng> later spelt <gai>foirfe,<eng> whence the late adjective <gai>foirbthech<eng> “aged” for which, as its only examples, DIL has quotations from Carswell 743, 769, 775. The nom. pl., despite the spellings in -idhe, -ighe, might imply a noun <gai>foirfeach,<eng> as may the pl. <gai>foirfidh<eng> in CC 373 b 4, but the other plural cases cannot do so. An alternative explanation would be to derive the noun from the secondary verb <gai>foirbthigidir<eng> “finishes, makes perfect”, as a spelling of the participle <gai>foirfighthe.<eng> Cf. too the noun <gai>foirbthidecht<eng> “moral perfection, holiness”, beside <gai>foirbthecht.<eng>

704 <gai>bhus cosmhula … rēna gcosmhala:<eng> in the first case “suitable” as parallel to <gai>cubhaidh,<eng> in the second “likely”. Carswell realistically omits the phrase “if there be choice” (of candidates for the vacancy) but retains the examination by ministers and elders;


[TD 131]

the ‘two or three’ which, in the English, refers to the candidates, he makes into the examining board.

708 <gai>an bfuil … an a bfuilid:<eng> if an a is not a misprint it may represent a doubling of the interrogative particle an, with a representing the weakened form in which the consonant is lost.

715 <gai>gabhaid sgēla a bheatha:<eng> ‘they enquire of his life’; but in the Gaelic the verb is probably imperative, as is the parallel <gai>tugaid<eng> 719. Formally <gai>beatha<eng> can be gen. sg. <gai>beathadh<eng> (from <gai>beatha<eng> “life”) or <gai>beatha<eng> (from <gai>bioth<eng> “world, age”). The former makes the better sense, and the spelling may be corrected, but cf. note on 1286.

728 <gai>mar:<eng> “if not, unless”; a number of forms and spellings occur, <gai>mura bfuil<eng> 363, <gai>mara bfuil<eng> 368, <gai>mur fhuaidheam agas mur fhīr-cheanglum<eng> 531, <gai>mur thograidīs<eng> 2383, <gai>mur dhearrna tú<eng> 2701, as well as <gai>muna dhearna sé<eng> 806, <gai>muna faghthar<eng> 2418, and <gai>mun saora tusa<eng> 1002, <gai>mun dēntar<eng> 1672, <gai>mun roibhe sé<eng> 1770, for which see CDIL s.v. 3 má.

732 <gai>tar ēis an mheadhōin laoi:<eng> ‘at afternoon, the sermon ended’; clearly two services are envisaged as this and the phrase <gai>do chum na searmōna maidne<eng> 729 show. Whereas the BCO has ‘framing his sermon, or some part therof, to the setting forth of his duty’ the Gaelic says that part of the morning sermon should relate to the election of the minister.

737 <gai>ag cuimhniughadh …:<eng> ‘with request of such things as shall be necessary for his office’, but the Gaelic means “remembering his office according as he is elected.”

750 <gai>eidir siad:<eng> but also <gai>eatorra féin<eng> 781 “amongst themselves”.

751 <gai>Ag cruindiughadh …:<eng> in rendering the sentence ‘In assembling the people, neither they without the ministers, nor the ministers without them, may attempt anything’, Carswell has omitted the phrase in italics.

755 <gai>maille ré rēsún agas ré toil cháich:<eng> ‘by the consent of the rest’. At 2502 <gai>résún<eng> is similarly paired with <gai>tuigse<eng> ‘discretion’, in each case with some straining of the meaning.

767 <gai>go dītheallach:<eng> for other examples of palatal th for ch see in the Glossary, s.vv. <gai>deith, dītheall, fithead, fitheadmhadh,<eng> and cf. note 3194.

771 <gai>ar eagla go gcaithfidhe daondacht na ndaoine maith …:<eng> ‘having ever a diligent care, that the charity of godly men be not wasted upon loiterers and idle vagabonds’, with the italicised


[TD 132]

phrase omitted. Note the variety of senses given to <gai>daondacht,<eng> “humanity” (i.e. human-ness) at 473-5, 1832, “humanity” (i.e. humane-ness and so charity) here, and <gai>daondacht bheathadh<eng> 3871 (with <gai>fascadh<eng>) rendering ‘harbour’, i.e. “protection, refuge”. In <gai>maithe,<eng> as originally printed, we have an instance of the common plural form of the adjective being used instead of the specifically gen. pl. one; cf. <gai>na ndaoine easlána<eng> 2500 instead of <gai>na ndaoine n-easlán,<eng> and in the Dean of Lismore’s Book ban oyga, i.e. <gai>ban óga<eng> as a gen. pl.

777 Carswell omits the introductory justification for the weekly meeting and proceeds from ‘it is ordained that every Thursday the ministers and elders in their assembly or consistory …’. He has omitted to mention the elders and has added the deacons and the whole congregation, thus transforming what was destined to be the beginnings of presbytery into a congregational meeting.

779 <gai>gacha Dar-daoin:<eng> note the adverbial genitive <gai>gacha,<eng> and cf. <gai>uair gacha seachtmhuine<eng> 810 “once a week”.

783 <gai>na Phairisidh:<eng> also 2465, in both cases printed with unlenited p-; it is uncertain whether this is due to the general reluctance to express the lenition of p-, or whether Carswell preferred a form of the word akin to the <gai>Pairisíneach<eng> which CDIL quotes from TSh and the Irish New Testament. The lenition is hesitantly adopted in this edition on the perhaps inadequate ground that Sc.G. prefers <gai>Phairiseach,<eng> of which this is the pl.

783 <gai>neoch ré bfaicthear:<eng> “to whom is visible”, “who can see”; the impersonal is virtually passive here.

792-5 In this list Carswell omits ‘papistry’ before ‘schism’, <gai>sgandail<eng> renders ‘blasphemy’, and he reduces ‘unlawful games, with such like’ to <gai>imbeirt,<eng> possibly gambling.

799 <gai>neamh-chūram …:<eng> ‘negligence, as well in his sermons and in studying the Scriptures, as in all other things concerning his vocation; scurrility, flattering, lying, back-biting, wanton words, deceit, covetousness, taunting, dissolution in apparel, gesture and other his doings’, but Carswell omits the italicised portions, and makes backbiting an offence only when <gai>minic.<eng>

802 <gai>a ngibluibh:<eng> ‘in apparel’; cf. IGT Decl. § 17 gibal, Dinneen giobal, Dwelly <gai>gioball,<eng> but CDIL gibal, i.e. <gai>giobhal;<eng> presumably it is the scarcity of examples collected for CDIL, coupled with the modern Irish usage, that limits the meaning to “piece of cloth”.


[TD 133]

In the Metrical Psalms (1694) 102.26 we find <gai>Biaidh iadsan fòs ar teirachdain / mar ghiobal sean gu léir; / Feuch claochluigh tù mar thruscan iad,<eng> in which the parallelism of <gai>giobal<eng> and <gai>truscan<eng> shows that Carswell’s usage is not eccentric. The Psalms moreover are supposed to be <gai>do réir na Heabhra,<eng> and the Hebrew here is usually translated “clothing”.

804 <gai>a bfuland:<eng> similarly <gai>do fhulaind<eng> 1538, with -nd for -ng.

811 <gai>agā mhīniughadh agas agā thuigsin:<eng> the concord is not with <gai>cuid,<eng> but with the nearer noun <gai>Sgribhtūir.<eng>

816 <gai>nī sa mó:<eng> Carswell has <gai>ní is<eng> before the predicative and adverbial comparatives of other adjectives, as 786, but regularly <gai>nī sa mó<eng> and <gai>nī sa lia.<eng> The normal contruction is with is, the present of the copula, or better with <gai>as,<eng> its relative form; sa appears (CDIL s.v. 7 sa) to go back to the OIr construction of the “bigger and bigger” type, OIr mó assa mó (GOI § 377), where assa geminates a following consonant, but Keating has ní sa mhó with lenition, as does Carswell at 2092, 2320. Ní sa looks like a contamination of these two constructions, but CDIL s.v. 1 ní 40.46 assumes níosa is derived directly from ní as. CC has only ni is.

817 <gai>eidir thuatadhaibh nó ghaisgeadhachaibh:<eng> see note on 575. For the first noun see CDIL s.v. túatae, where 348.15 under the gloss “rustic, ignorant person” the dat. pl. <gai>do na tuataighibh<eng> is quoted from Donlevy and equated with <gai>tuatadhaibh,<eng> by the editors, while at 348.8 we have <gai>do na tuataibh<eng> “to the laity”. For the second see CDIL s.v. gaiscedach “one bearing arms, a warrior, a champion”; this quotation is not used by CDIL in either article. A contrast between the two terms is certain, and “amongst commonalty or gentry” fits the sense best.

824 At this point the ordinary order of service begins. BCO, and consequently Carswell, omit the first confession of the Geneva book (Laing IV 179-181).

831 <gai>a bfiadhnuise do chumhachtaibh diadha-sa:<eng> “before thy divine majesty”, a regular meaning of <gai>cumhachta<eng> in Carswell, e.g. 964.

834 <gai>a bpeacadh …:<eng> the whole series of five nouns corresponds to ‘in sin and iniquity’, and the words ‘so that in us there is no goodness’ are not translated.

849 <gai>atāmaoid … agad ghuidhe-se:<eng> the development of this characteristically Scottish (and early Manx) construction is attri-


[TD 134]

buted (ID 132) to the acquisition of future meaning by the present-tense forms, which in turn depends (ID 222) on the reduction of the f-formative of the future to -h-, followed by the dialectal loss of the -h-. See now Professor Greene’s criticism of this approach in Proceedings of the Second International Congress of Celtic Studies 1963 (Cardiff, 1966) 135-6. The argument is in any case applicable only to verbs having an f-future: in the irregular verbs it is the Irish present that corresponds formally to the Scottish Gaelic and Manx future, and it would be more natural to expect one and the same process to apply to all verbs. Cf. CDIL s.v. oc 82.55-64 and III. Carswell usually has the shortened forms <gai>’gud,<eng> with <gai>’gut<eng> before a vowel.

861 <gai>an lā bhudh fhearr iad:<eng> “the day that they were best”, i.e. “even at their best”.

864 <gai>tugabhair:<eng> 2 pl. preterite, here a plural of respect.

874 <gai>ris an Mac mōr mīorbhuileach:<eng> cf. 292, where a hyphen might be inserted; but as it stands each noun has two alliterating epithets and the same consideration applies here, confirmed by the absence of lenition in the second adjective. But at 1406 it is necessary to admit the compound adjective.

877 From BCO, an addition to GB; see Laing VI 294-6.

889 <gai>ar gcoinsiasa:<eng> and so normally, as in Des. and CC, but occasionally with -n- restored. It seems to have ousted the native MIr cubus almost entirely. The compounded form of the latter, however, co-cubus (CIL), is found in TSh and the Irish Prayer Book in the form cogúas, and in Sc.G. as <gai>cogais.<eng>

905 <gai>an Rīoghacht:<eng> i.e. Scotland. Note the concord <gai>a n-aingidheachta<eng> and <gai>do ghnāthuidheadar<eng> 906-7, which are with the plural notion “the inhabitants of Scotland”.

908 <gai>na bPriondsadh:<eng> the first edition of BCO has “Princess” here, a reference to Queen Mary, later changed to “Princes”, as in Carswell.

919 <gai>ar bfaicsin dúinn go …:<eng> “when we saw that …”, but there is nothing corresponding in the English and the phrase, which Carswell uses several times, is rather “seeing that …” as a causal conjunction; cf. 1314, 2897, 2944. The English construction appears to date from the early sixteenth century, but even when it stands in his original Carswell does not always imitate it: cf. <gai>ar dtuigsin dúinn go ndearrna an Tighearna an uiread-sin do thrōcaire oraind<eng> ‘seeing


[TD 135]

that our Lord has dealt thus mercifully with us’ 2196; similarly 2200, and <gai>ō tāid na h-uile chrēatūire ’gut aithne<eng> ‘seeing that all creatures do acknowledge thee’ 2248. A particularly clear case is <gai>ar bfaicsin duit-se gurab í do thoil<eng> ‘seeing that it hath pleased thee’ 2607, where “when you saw that it was your will” would be absurd. Literal meaning at 1741.

923 <gai>ag cur at adhaigh:<eng> ‘rebel’, a rather stronger sense than that of “oppose” or “contradict” found in TSh and Des. Cf. <gai>gan chur ’na h-adhaidh<eng> 1345.

931 <gai>an tan … d’allmhurrchuibh:<eng> Carswell omits ‘by our foolishness’.

933 <gai>nī fhuil comhnuidhe ort … acht ag toirbheart:<eng> “thou ceasest not to give”.

940 <gai>an chomhdāil chogaidh:<eng> ‘the whispering of sedition’; Gaelic rather “mustering for battle”, though <gai>comhdhāil<eng> could perhaps mean “plotting”.

944 <gai>doineand agas droch-tsīona na h-aimsire:<eng> the lines 939 to 947 are marked in BCO ‘These clauses … may be used, or any of them as occasion serveth’ in a marginal note, so that there need be no specific reference intended by their inclusion in the Gaelic version. Calderwood (II 248) says: In Januarie (1564), upon the 20th day therof, the rain falling freezed so vehementlie, that the ground was like a shott of yce. The fowles of the aire deed, and might not flee. In the same moneth the sea stood still, neither flowing nor ebbing the space of twentie-foure houres.

947 <gai>lom-lán: lom<eng> could at the best have only an intensive force in this compound, but even so its meaning is too nearly the opposite of <gai>lán;<eng> according to CDIL s.v. <gai>lomnán, lom-lán<eng> is not what it seems, but a form of <gai>imlán.<eng>

967 <gai>buaidh dtroda:<eng> “victory”, with <gai>troda,<eng> gen. of <gai>troid<eng> “fight” as at 816; the exceptional nasalisation occurs because <gai>buaidh<eng> is acc. sg. or even because it was once neuter.

973 This ‘confession of our sins, and petitions, made unto God in the time of our extreme troubles’ was, like the preceding, an addition in BCO (Laing VI 296-7).

979 <gai>ag dēnamh umhla agas prósdāla:<eng> ‘we prostrate ourselves’; <gai>prosdáil<eng> is noted in CDIL N-O-P 207.73 from this passage only. It also occurs below 2591.

979 <gai>a bfiadhnuise do chathrach cumhachtaighe-se:<eng> ‘before the


[TD 136]

throne of thy majesty’; cf. note 831. Carswell apparently makes no distinction between <gai>cathair<eng> “city” and <gai>cathaoir<eng> “throne”; for the gen. sg. of the latter Keating (TSh 6799) has cathaoireach.

981 <gai>fīor-allmhurrdha:<eng> cf. 1006 <gai>allmhurraidh.<eng> The foreigners are, of course, the French, expelled in July 1560.

984 <gai>rīoghruidh:<eng> not strictly the pl. of <gai>rí,<eng> for which Carswell has <gai>rīghthe<eng> 83, but a fem. sg. collective <gai>ríoghradh,<eng> whence the gen. sg. <gai>na rīghruidhe Albanuidhe<eng> 28, and similarly 179, 1206. The nom. sg. here with the form of the acc.-dat. occurs also in a passage of Keating (TSh 7875). Cf. too the Sc.G. pl. <gai>rìghre, rìghrean.<eng>

992 <gai>atá … ar lán-līonadh:<eng> “have filled”; on the history of the periphrastic perfect with <gai>ar<eng> (from <gai>iar<eng>) with a verbal noun, a typically East Gaelic feature, see ID 135, 234-5. In Carswell, as in early Manx and on its rare appearances in Irish, ar nasalises. Further examples at 1189, 1442.

1008 <gai>gidh bé mar tá:<eng> equivalent to <gai>gé tá.<eng>

1010 <gai>ar son ghlōire th’anma féin:<eng> gen. sg. of <gai>ainm<eng> here and at 1325, but Carswell has identical forms for the gen. sg. and gen. pl. (<gai>anmand<eng>) of both <gai>ainm<eng> and <gai>anam.<eng> The gen. sg. <gai>ainme<eng> seems to be a later creation.

1012 <gai>eadruind:<eng> “amongst us”; similarly <gai>eadraind<eng> 1016.

1014 <gai>iondas go bfeicfidh an saoghal … agas go dtuigidh gach aon:<eng> “that all the world may know”; for such pairings of future and present subjunctive see the Introduction.

1021 Before this prayer Carswell has omitted the rubric: ‘This done, the people sing a psalm all together in a plain tune; which ended, the Minister prayeth for the assistance of God’s Holy Spirit as the same shall move his heart, and so proceedeth to the sermon, using after the sermon this prayer following, or such like.’ Cf. note 1399.

1024 <gai>atāmaoid … dār lēigean féin … agas ag ar dtoirrneamh:<eng> cf. note 215. For <gai>toirrneamh<eng> see CDIL s.v. tairniud.

1028 <gai>do pfrēmhughadh:<eng> for initial p- in this word cf. Keating’s History II 5792. Carswell elsewhere has f-, as 577, 621, 2601. The spelling pf- for lenited p- is unusual, though fp- and fph- are not uncommon; e.g. CC 16 b 5 <gai>do fhulaing an fpháis fa Fpuinge Fphioláid,<eng> probably taken direct from the Irish Prayer Book ff. IIi verso, kkk2, and LLL 2, which also has an Fphosda f. NNn verso. Possibly


[TD 137]

from the same source in the Metrical Psalms (1659) 18.32 <gai>Se Dia a fphasgas me le neart, / is ni mo shlighe re.<eng>

1032 <gai>i ndeigh-ithir: ithir<eng> is etymologically “cornland”, from <gai>ith;<eng> cf. <gai>iothlann.<eng>

1037 <gai>inar n-iarratasaibh:<eng> M’L read the original as mar, but I read the facsimile of the Pierpont Morgan copy as inar. Professor Matheson suggested um ar, which would be a palaeographically straightforward correction, though um occurs only once elsewhere in the text. Carswell generally uses fá to introduce this kind of phrase, e.g. <gai>fā spioraid an ghliocais … do neartughadh … and<eng> 30-32, <gai>aontadhach … fā chūrsadh do dhēnamh<eng> 672, <gai>fāna daoinibh … d’iompōdh<eng> 1073, <gai>fā chroidhe gach uile rīogh … do chongbhāil<eng> 1090-92, <gai>fād thrōcaire … d’fhoillseachadh<eng> 1117, <gai>fād tseirbhīs … do dhēnamh<eng> 1346, and <gai>fár n-iarratasaibh<eng> is what we should expect here. As this is a construction in which fo has displaced imm (cf. DIL s.v. fo 173.67-) it may be that we should read mar and analyse it as imm + ar, with apocope.

1043 <gai>a bfēgmhuis do chuidigh-se:<eng> so too at 2546, but more correctly <gai>cuidighthe<eng> 1120, and most commonly <gai>cuidighe<eng> 251 etc. It may be significant that both these shortened forms have the augens -se.

1052 <gai>Mēdaidh ar gcreidimh-ne:<eng> see note 495.

1076 <gai>go nglan-tuigsin t’fhīrinde neamhdha-sa:<eng> ‘to the pure understanding and knowledge of thy heavenly truth’; cf. note 644 for <gai>go n-<eng> “with”, but this is ruled out here by the context, and n- must be an error.

1093 <gai>agas go h-āiridhe …:<eng> from here to 1104 we have an insertion in BCO (Laing VI 298) replacing the prayers for the city of Geneva in the first version (Laing IV 183-4).

1096 <gai>ar mBanrīoghna agas ar bPriondsa:<eng> i.e. Queen Mary and the future James VI.

1097 <gai>futhadh:<eng> cf. <gai>futhaibh<eng> 1218, and DIL s.v. fo 167.45-53. The first of these forms is the original accusative (with unhistorical -dh), the second the original dative, but without distinction of sense. Cf. note 1277.

1114 After <gai>fā dheōidh<eng> a short passage of the Geneva Book was struck out in BCO. M’L prints the original text on p. 69 ‘And as we be bound … destroy thy flock.’ BCO then proceeds, ‘And


[TD 138]

finally, O Lord God, most merciful Father, we most humbly beseech thee to show thy great mercies …’.

1132 For another text of the Lord’s Prayer see 1574-80, and cf. CC p. 71 for a very similar rendering, differing chiefly in the use of augentia, and with pl. for sg. <gai>neamh, naomhtha<eng> for <gai>beandaighthe,<eng> and <gai>cíulan<eng> for <gai>lēig.<eng> The Irish Prayer Book version, f. A2, runs: Ar nathairne atá ar neamh, náomhthar thainmsi. Tigeadh do righachd. Déntar do thoil, ar talmhain amhuil do níthear ar neamh. Tabhair dhuinn aniu ar narán laoitheamhuil, 7 maith dhuinn ar bfhiacha, amhuil mhaithmidne dár bfhéitheamhnaibh féin. Agus na tréoraigh chum buaigheartha sinn, achd sáor sinn ó nolc. The final doxology does not occur. At 1132 <gai>go mo<eng> is a dittography for <gai>go ma;<eng> 1133 <gai>dod ríghe,<eng> but correctly <gai>do<eng> at 1575.

1146 With this version of the Creed cf. CC p. 4; the differences are again slight: the insertion of <gai>do crochadh<eng> in CC, <gai>go hifreand<eng> instead of <gai>a n-ifreand, do éirghidh ó bhas a gciond an treas lá, atá anois na shuidhe, comhchoitchionn<eng> for <gai>go h-uilidhe, cumand, marthanach<eng> for <gai>suthain.<eng> Both agree on <gai>eisēirghe cholla<eng> (or <gai>chodla) na marbh.<eng> The CC version is extremely close to and probably derived from the Irish Prayer Book f. B2 verso, with which it agrees wherever it differs from Carswell except for the Prayer Book’s <gai>ó mharbhuibh (= ó bhás), shuidheas (= atā … na shuidhe),<eng> and <gai>éiseirghe na colla (= eiséirghe chodla na marbh).<eng>

1154 <gai>tiucfas:<eng> CC <gai>thiocfas,<eng> but Irish Prayer Book tiocfas.

1156 <gai>andsa Sbiorad:<eng> in no version of this last sentence is the preposition repeated before each noun in turn.

1158 <gai>colla:<eng> by assimilation from <gai>colna,<eng> gen. sg. of <gai>colann.<eng>

1161 <gai>na Psalm:<eng> “a Psalm’, but the Gaelic has the plural.

1163 <gai>dār mbeandachadh:<eng> cf. note 282. In the Gaelic the pronouns are 1st. pers. pl., in the English 2nd. pers. pl. in the Geneva Book (Laing IV 185), but 1st. pl. in BCO (Laing VI 298).

1167 <gai>a tsīthchāimhe:<eng> “his peace”; Carswell has the more familiar <gai>sīothchāna<eng> at 488 (in the sense of “reconciliation”).
