This form may represent a compound of the synonyms <gai>síoth<eng> and <gai>támh,<eng> but in view of the other form of the gen. sg. <gai>na gnáth-tsíthchāine<eng> 3863, this may be only a misprint.

1172 This concluded the order for the ordinary service in the Geneva Book, except for a direction that it need not be followed verbatim, and for special prayers for use in time of plague, famine,


[TD 139]

pestilence, war, or such like (Laing IV 186). It then proceeded to the order of Baptism. The BCO, however, inserts new matter here, introduced by a rubric which Carswell omits: ‘these prayers that follow are used in the French church of Geneva. The first serveth for Sunday after the sermon, and the other that followeth is said upon Wednesday which is the day of common prayer.’

1177 <gai>atāmaoid:<eng> perhaps more correctly <gai>ó támaoid<eng> (cf. 1531 note), and so parallel to <gai>ó dho gheallais<eng> 1175, though in the English “since” is not repeated.

1182 <gai>atámaoid …<eng> 1192 <gai>atámaoid …:<eng> ‘we, therefore, … we, I say, …’; the subject has become unduly separated from the main verb and makes a fresh start.

1187 <gai>an mhéid agas atá …:<eng> ‘forasmuch as our faith is grounded …’; but usually after an (or in) mhéid agas Carswell has <gai>go,<eng> as <gai>go raibhe<eng> 469, 845, and <gai>go bfuilmaoid<eng> 1067.

1200 <gai>do rēir:<eng> dat. of <gai>riar:<eng> as a phrase-preposition usually “according to”, “as regards” (cf. <gai>dā rēir sin<eng> 1215 “accordingly”), but here in the sense “obedient to”; cf. CDIL s.v. I ríar 59.3.

1210 <gai>ó aimsir go h-aimsir:<eng> here, as at 1240, exactly renders the English ‘from time to time’, and has the sense that it then had, viz. “continually”, a usage confined, according to NED, to the sixteenth century. At 1312 where the English phrase recurs, Carswell has <gai>gach én-lā.<eng> For another example of the sense “continually”, cf. the Irish Prayer Book f. FFf verso, ar míghníomha ró iomdha, noch dho rindeamar go ró adhúathmhar ar feadh ar mbheatha uile “our manifold sins and wickedness, which we from time to time most grievously have committed”.

1243 <gai>na mac-dtīre:<eng> “of the wolves”; cf. 1299 and CDIL s.v. mac 7.60.

1253 <gai>do bhí roimhe so a láimh:<eng> ‘have been hitherto holden captive’.

1260 <gai>na daoine dā dtugais do ghrāsaibh … do bhriathar:<eng> ‘they whom thou hast already endued with thy grace, and illuminated their hearts with the knowledge of thy word’; but the Gaelic is rather “those to whom thou hast granted by grace that their hearts should be illuminated and opened to understand thy word”. Do ghrāsaibh is “by grace” not “thy grace”, since Carswell never elsewhere uses the -ibh ending except for the dat. pl. Cf. Calder,


[TD 140]

Gaelic Grammar, pp. 79-80. If one wishes to retain ‘thy’, then <gai>dod ghrāsaibh<eng> would be an easy correction.

1270 <gai>triobalōid:<eng> but gen. sg. <gai>trioblōide<eng> 1108, and <gai>triobhlōid<eng> 1603. The -a- may be a svarabhakti vowel, but the -bh- has some historical justification; cf. CDIL s.v. treblait, whence MIr treabhluid, and also triblóid, a later borrowing of Latin tribulatio, as shown by the unlenited -b-, but with the suffix assimilated to that of words like OIr oróit, tríndóit, in which the -ó- reflects the British rounding of Celtic and Latin ā, and so marks them as taken from Latin orātio, trinitātem, through British. Since the evidence thus associates -ó- with unlenited -b-, it seems proper to correct to <gai>trioblōid<eng> at 1603, but this spelling may also represent the older treabhlaid, ? triobhlaid.

1271 <gai>amhgar:<eng> so here and at 1275, but <gai>amhghar<eng> 1297 and thereafter. Dinneen, TSh, Des. have amhgar, and Modern Irish angar, but Dwelly only <gai>àmhghar.<eng>

1275 <gai>corparrdha:<eng> cf. <gai>corparradha<eng> 1921, against the usual <gai>corporrdha.<eng>

1277 <gai>tabhairt orrtha …<eng> 1278 <gai>teacht orrthuibh …<eng> 1280 <gai>tabhairt orthuibh:<eng> similarly <gai>foillsigh … orra<eng> 1591, but <gai>foillsiughadh do ghrāidh orthuibh<eng> 1599; orrtha, orra (OIr forru) is the acc. form, orthuibh (OIr foraib) the dat. A distinction of form remains but not one of meaning.

1286 <gai>na n-Ainteadhcrīosd:<eng> it is not clear why Carswell here uses the gen. pl., but elsewhere, 620, 1616 (the same phrase as here) the sg. The spelling is very variable and the omission of t- at 2937 suggests that the word was not very well established in Gaelic. For other examples cf. CDIL s.v. Ainticríst.

1286 <gai>uireasbhuidh beatha:<eng> ‘lack this food of life’; Carswell uses <gai>biadh,<eng> gen. <gai>bídh<eng> for “food” but also occasionally <gai>beatha,<eng> gen. <gai>beathadh,<eng> e.g. 172 (with reference to manna), at 3808, and 3830 <gai>biadh agar beatha.<eng> Cf. CIL s.v. betha (2). For Carswell the -dh of the gen. sg. seems to have been silent, for he uses beatha as gen. at 715 note, 1913, 1919, and no doubt here too. After the nom. fem. sg. we should expect <gai>bheathadh.<eng>

1295 <gai>cumhanta:<eng> gen. of the verbal noun <gai>cungnamh, congnamh<eng> “help”.

1299 <gai>na madadh-alta:<eng> “wolves”, lit. wild dogs.

1302 <gai>fā dheōidh:<eng> although these words come at the end of a paragraph which ends in the middle of a line at the bottom of the


[TD 141]

page in the original edition, so that there is no ambiguity about the sentence division, they should probably have been printed on the line below as a catchword to be repeated at the head of f. F2, for the new paragraph opens ‘Finally, O Lord God, …’.

1304 <gai>an mhéid atamaoid:<eng> cf. the current Irish Prayer Book (1965) p. 9 an mhéid atá anseo i láthair “as many as are here present”, and the Scottish book (1794) pp. 2, 14 <gai>a mheid ’s ata ’n so lathair,<eng> but not the Irish book of 1609. See too <gai>an mhéid do-rindeamar … d’ēgōir<eng> 1408 “all the iniquity we have done”. Cf. CDIL s.v. méit II (e).

1307 <gai>agas do chomaoineachadh a Chuirp:<eng> in BCO there is a marginal note ‘If the Lord’s Supper be ministered, then is here added this clause: And to celebrate his holy Supper’.

1315 <gai>nach maireand:<eng> -and was in Irish the conjunct present ending, eventually ousting the independent -idh (ID 132), and although general in Carswell and CC has not survived in East Gaelic. There is no trace in Manx, and in Sc.G. it survives only in this phrase <gai>nach maireand<eng> “the late”, lit. “who does not survive”. The structural ambiguity of <gai>maireann<eng> in this phrase led to its being construed as an adjective once the verbal form had become obsolete, and it is so used in Metrical Psalm 61.6 <gai>Saogh’l maireann agus aimsir chian.<eng> For the verb cf. CDIL s.v. maraid.

1325 <gai>is oighri ar gach ēn-onōir é:<eng> ‘as it is worthy of all honour’; an unusual spelling (perhaps for <gai>oirigh,<eng> or influenced by <gai>oighre<eng> “heir”) for <gai>airidhe,<eng> for which see note 41. The last section of this prayer is based on the petitions of the Lord’s Prayer, as is indicated marginally in BCO.

1332 <gai>agā n-áirthear thú:<eng> ‘in such sort that thou mayest be counted’; agā n- can only be the relative form of the prep. ag, i.e. “at whom” or “by whom”, but we have to go too far back in the sentence to find a plausible antecedent for this to be a likely interpretation. In view of the English it seems more likely that we should correct aga n- to as go n-. The verb <gai>áirthear<eng> is apparently to be taken as from the verbal noun <gai>áireamh,<eng> stem áirmh-, as in 1st. pers. sg. present <gai>āirbhim<eng> and 2nd. sg. preterite <gai>do áirimh tú;<eng> the -mh- is also omitted in the impersonal conditional <gai>áirfidhe,<eng> but this is understandable before -f-. The loss of -mh- in the present impersonal is harder to justify, and we may emend the text to read <gai>as go n-áirmhthear thú.<eng>


[TD 142]

1334 <gai>cloidheamh:<eng> but ‘sceptre’ in BCO.

1367 <gai>atāmuid ’gud ghuidhe-se …:<eng> ‘we beseech thee, pardon us our innumerable offences … and forgive us so freely that death and sin may hereafter have no title against us.’ Some such phrase as <gai>agas maith dhúinn go saor,<eng> might be inserted after <gai>imarcacha<eng> to correspond to the English but it is not necessary to the sense or the grammar and only repeats the idea of the previous phrase.

1377 <gai>sgáth agas díon agas dīdin:<eng> ‘wealth’, in the older sense of “good, profit, advantage”.

1387 <gai>lēd t’intind:<eng> cf. <gai>dod t’eadar-ghuidhe-se<eng> 2545, <gai>dod honorughadh-sa<eng> 3176, for a similar double expression of the possessive.

1389 <gai>fearrdha:<eng> i.e. <gai>feardha,<eng> adjective from <gai>fear;<eng> for the -rr- see note 1.

1399 At this point Carswell inserts a short prayer, apparently of his own composition, or at least not taken from the Geneva Book or BCO. He may have thought it desirable to give a specimen of the kind of prayer that would be appropriate after the psalm before the sermon, as the rubric (see note 1021), while prescribing a prayer, offers no suggestion as to wording.

1402 <gai>d’fhosgladh agas d’fhoillsiughadh:<eng> cf. <gai>ar gcroidheadha d’fhoillsiudhadh<eng> 2913 ‘lighten our hearts’; Carswell elsewhere uses <gai>foillsiughadh<eng> to mean “show, illustrate, proclaim, reveal, preach”, all of which are consistent with its derivation from <gai>follus.<eng> Here we should expect rather <gai>soillsiughadh,<eng> but in neither of these places is emendation on the basis of f misread for long s possible, for then we should also have to correct d’ to do, and in this passage we should also spoil the alliteration.

1410 <gai>do lēigean lind gan dīoghaltus:<eng> cf. TSh glossary s.v. léigim le “allow one to have”.

1424 This prayer, which Carswell allows to be used on any occasion, is introduced in BCO by the rubric: ‘This prayer following, is to be said after the sermon, on the day which is appointed for common prayer: and it is very proper for our state and time, to move us to true repentance, and to turn back God’s sharp rods which yet threaten us.’

1434 <gai>ag dēnamh fhiadhnuise [’nar n-adhaidh]:<eng> ‘bear witness against us’.

1447 <gai>gach mēid mar mhothuidhemuid … is mōide dhlighmuid …:<eng> ‘the more we feel … so much the more we should …’; for


[TD 143]

this use of <gai>gach méid<eng> cf. CDIL s.v. méit III (d). <gai>Móide<eng> is <gai>mó<eng> plus <gai>de<eng> “more on that account” and so “the more”, sometimes treated by Sc.G. grammarians as an extra degree of comparison, e.g. Calder, Gaelic Grammar § 94, “second comparative”.

1450 <gai>do mhōr-thrōcaire-se sin:<eng> “that great mercy of thine”, with both possessive <gai>do<eng> (and augens -se) and demonstrative <gai>sin.<eng> The combination of these two is not common in Gaelic (cf. CDIL s.v. 1 sin I (d), but is quite spontaneous here as the English has ‘thy sovereign bounty’.

1477 <gai>gur thimpōidheamar:<eng> normally the verbal noun is <gai>impódh<eng> rather than the late <gai>timpódh<eng> (on which see CDIL s.v. do-immpói), condemned by IGT Verbs § 64, but here required by the alliteration with <gai>tilleadh.<eng> Forms of this verb are found in the Irish Linguistic Atlas, map 178, at points 56-9, 61, 69, 70, 76-7; does this give a clue to where in Ireland Carswell or his bardic teachers might have studied?

1478 <gai>a lenmhuin:<eng> a may be the equivalent of <gai>do<eng> or <gai>ag,<eng> but probably the former, as a’ for ag is normal in Carswell only before velars.

1480 <gai>dot th’ainm:<eng> even in the original spelling <gai>hainm<eng> we have a double possessive here, as in <gai>ad t’athair<eng> and <gai>ad t’fhear-dídin<eng> 3113. When not lenited, t’ is the usual prevocalic form, and similarly -t in combination with prepositions, but -d before consonants (though not invariably, e.g. 1333, 1481). It makes little difference whether we write <gai>dot ainm<eng> or <gai>do t’ainm,<eng> but the analogy of the forms written before consonants, as <gai>dod ghliocas<eng> 1125, <gai>lēd bhriathruibh<eng> 1484, suggests we should prefer dot.

1515 <gai>do thuill siad-san … do thuilleadar-san:<eng> synonymous analytic and synthetic verbal forms, presumably as a stylistic variation.

1519 <gai>ar n-aodhaire:<eng> more correctly <gai>aoghaire,<eng> cf. CDIL s.v. oegaire; etymologically speaking “shepherd” as distinct from “cowherd”, i.e. <gai>buachaill,<eng> though here paired with the latter.

1522 <gai>fā dheōidh:<eng> should follow the next agas, i e ‘finally, because thou art our God’; cf. note 1302.

1531 <gai>ō tāmaoid:<eng> printed <gai>atamaoid,<eng> but BCO has ‘considering that we call’; cf. too note 1177.

1542 <gai>na coinsiansa ar a bfuil eire:<eng> printed <gai>na gcoinsiansa a bfuil eire,<eng> but BCO has four parallel phrases, viz. ‘the heavy souls and comfortless, the humble hearts, the consciences oppressed and


[TD 144]

laden with the grievous burden of their sins, and [who] therefore thirst after thy grace.’ Alternatively we could read <gai>’na gcoinsiansaibh ar a bfuil eire,<eng> parallel to <gai>’na gcroidheadhaibh umhla,<eng> as Carswell seems to have intended to make some change in the English construction.

1550 <gai>nī raibhe do thruime nō do mhēd a bpeacadh:<eng> ‘were their enormities and sins never so grievous’ ; cf. note 149.

1553 <gai>gur impāidh tú:<eng> cf. CDIL: s.v. imm-sói for -ái- as well as Carswell’s usual -ói-. The -o- is never marked long and if Carswell did not pronounce it so it would not matter whether he wrote -aidh or -oidh or -uidh, for all would sound -í or possibly -ich. Since in Sc.G. this is identical with verbs having -achadh as their verbal noun, the modern form is <gai>iompachadh.<eng>

1562 <gai>chumairce agas chuimridhe:<eng> ‘refuge’; beside <gai>cumairce<eng> Carswell also has <gai>comairce<eng> 258, but not coimirce as in TSh. The gen. sg. <gai>cuimridhe<eng> is obscure; it seems most likely to be the abstract from Dwelly’s <gai>cuimreach<eng> “assistant” (quoted from Armstrong’s Supplement), i.e. <gai>cuimrighe.<eng> Dinneen gives coimrighe, comraighe, merely as variant forms of coimirce; Dwelly has <gai>coimirc<eng> and <gai>coimric.<eng>

1595 <gai>rē plaidh … lē tindeas …:<eng> two cases of <gai>ré<eng> and four of <gai>lé,<eng> all parallel and with the same meaning. There is extensive confusion of the prepositions representing OIr fri and la in later Irish, and to a lesser extent in some forms of Sc.G. and Manx. Cf. in the note on 2163 the Irish <gai>maille ré creideamh … agus lé dóchus, lé plághaibh agus ré gallraighthibh,<eng> and below <gai>maille rēna … agas lēna …<eng> 2938.

1604 <gai>sobrōin:<eng> antonym of <gai>dobrón,<eng> and so paired with <gai>subhachas;<eng> since the simplex here is <gai>brón<eng> “grief” it is surprising that it takes no more than the ameliorative prefix so- to turn it into “pleasure”. The re-formation <gai>so-bhròn<eng> quoted by Dwelly from Armstrong gives more nearly the sense we might expect. Cf. CDIL s.v. 2 so- 309.15 for another example, and for the pairing of the corresponding adjectives, see IT IV i 2457 subach sobrónach.

1610 <gai>do nāmhad agas nāmhad t’fhīrinde:<eng> but ‘of their enemies and thine’.

1623 With the Order of Baptism we return to the Geneva Book.

1625 <gai>toirmisge:<eng> ‘not permitted’; similarly written at 803. Cf. CDIL s.v. do-airmesca 192.21, tairmisgthe with -th- inserted editorially; a phonetic rather than an etymological spelling.


[TD 145]

1629 <gai>mar phiseōgaibh nō mar ghliocas:<eng> ‘as charms or sorceries’; that such misuse of the consecrated bread was a real danger is confirmed by the final rubric in the Communion Order in the First Prayerbook of Edward VI (1549): “And although it be read in ancient writers, that the people many years past received at the priest’s hands the Sacrament of the body of Christ in their own hands, and no commandment of Christ to the contrary; yet forasmuch as they many times conveyed the same secretly away, kept it with them, and diversely abused it to superstition and wickedness; lest any such thing hereafter should be attempted, and that an uniformity might be used throughout the whole realm, it is thought convenient the people commonly receive the Sacrament of Christ’s body in their mouths, at the priest’s hand.” Other counsels prevailed, however, and from the Second Prayerbook (1552) onwards the rubric prescribes administration “to the people in their hands.”

1630 <gai>a bfāgbhāil:<eng> printed <gai>abfaghail,<eng> but renders ‘left [i.e. entrusted] to the congregation.’

1631 <gai>mar tséla aithrighe:<eng> “as seals of repentance”, but ‘as seals of the same [i.e. the Word]’.

1635 <gai>an fhiadhnuise:<eng> “the witness”, but ‘god-father’; similarly 1766 for ‘surety’, but for ‘god-father’ 1771 Carswell has <gai>an t-athair spioratālta.<eng>

1642 Geneva Book has simply ‘The answer’.

1648 <gai>cland ochta:<eng> ‘children by adoption’, cf. 1792 and CC 366 c 2 <gai>mac ochta,<eng> and 46 b 2 <gai>macacht ochta.<eng> In CDIL s.v. ucht 50.33 the only sense of mac ochta is “dear son”, like English “bosom friend”. The Scottish use as “adopted son” may imply some contrasting phrase such as <gai>mac bronn,<eng> or it may be influenced by the occurrence of phrases like <gai>do ghabh … Crīosd chuige … ’na ucht an chland bheag<eng> 1667, and <gai>ag faicsin bhur gcloinde agā ngabhāil a n-ucht an choimhthinōil<eng> 1741.

1651 <gai>céim:<eng> ‘generation’; cf. Shorter Catechism 1659 (CC, p. 248) <gai>go nuige an treas no an ceathramh cem no glun ghinealuigh,<eng> which is only a slightly modernised version of Carswell’s own translation in his catechism, below 3438. The words <gai>go nuige an mīle céim<eng> correspond to <gai>gus an mhíle genealach<eng> CC 155 b 3, and arise from an interpretation which understood “thousands” to refer to generations, not to individuals. Carswell in the catechism (like the Irish Prayer Book, LLL 2 verso, from which CC 217 b derives) does not


[TD 146]

follow this interpretation but has <gai>trócaire dona míltibh don druing agā mbia mo ghrádh<eng> 3439. Note the absence of a distinct ordinal form in <gai>an mīle céim;<eng> CDIL s.v. míle has no examples of this use, but only a single one s.v. mílmad. The English has ‘unto the thousand generation’, where thousand is both cardinal and ordinal, the modern thousandth being an analogical re-formation; cf. NED s.v. thousand 4, and, for a parallel, eighth, quotations 1477-1609.

1652 <gai>an ghearraidh-thimchill:<eng> similarly <gai>teascadh-timchill<eng> 3715; in such a compound <gai>timcheall<eng> (so spelt regularly in Carswell, <gai>timchiol<eng> in CC) is adverbial, but in these cases it is made gen. as if the object of <gai>gearradh<eng> and <gai>teascadh.<eng> CDIL has no examples of these compounds, but s.v. timchell has both t.-gerrad and t.-tescad. The Manx giarey-chymmylt, with subsequent development of the meaning “foreskin” for chymmylt (cf. Journal of the Manx Museum VII 63), is the closest parallel available.

1661 <gai>ach:<eng> the only instance of the modern form in contrast to Carswell’s usual <gai>acht,<eng> and presumably an oversight. ID 269 notes that ach is found in fifteenth-century Irish manuscripts; it is also the form in the earliest Manx, and is found in the Dean of Lismore’s Book.

1665 <gai>athair:<eng> but ‘either of the parents’ in the Geneva Book.

1668 <gai>an chland bheag:<eng> also 1670, with singular adjective in strict concord, but plural pronouns <gai>iad, leō.<eng>

1669 <gai>gurab do phobal Dé an chland bheag:<eng> ‘that infants be of the number of God’s people.’

1675 <gai>deifriughadh:<eng> “haste”; historical spelling <gai>deithbhriughadh.<eng>

1686 <gai>ar gcēdfadh tromdhoi-ne:<eng> ‘our dull senses’; cf. notes 495, 1052 for this effect of -ne. Similarly <gai>ar nádūir dhaonnai-ne<eng> 1837, <gai>ar ndeagh-bheathai-ne<eng> 3292.

1692 <gai>puindsiūn:<eng> cf. CC 262 b 7 and note. Professor Greene observes that -n- appears also in the Old Spanish form ponçoña (SGS X 247), but this is too remote a source to be likely, and the ending in -ūn points clearly to Norman French. The -n- is intrusive and late, the source being Latin potionem (cf. J. Corominas, Diccionario Crítico Etimológico da la Lengua Castellana, s.v. ponzoña) and it is probably no more than a coincidence that it should occur sporadically in the Romance languages and in Scottish Gaelic; there seem to be no examples in NED.

1694 <gai>deasgaidh:<eng> “dregs”; on the various forms of the word see


[TD 147]

CDIL s.v. descad. Dwelly gives <gai>deasgadh<eng> m. and <gai>deasgainn<eng> f., but it seems to be f. here to judge from the pronoun í 1956, which should, according to the English, refer to <gai>deasgaidh,<eng> and not, as it may in the Gaelic, to <gai>droch-nādūir.<eng>

1713 <gai>umarcach:<eng> usually <gai>i(o)marcach;<eng> cf. <gai>umchubhaidh<eng> 2242 beside the usual <gai>imchubhaidh,<eng> and perhaps <gai>iumad<eng> 1465 beside <gai>imad,<eng> and <gai>iumchur<eng> 3271 beside <gai>iomchur.<eng>

1714 <gai>agas an sgiath daingean do-leōnta a n-adhaidh bháis agas ifrind:<eng> GB has only ‘against sin, death and hell’, the rest “and the firm impenetrable shield” being Carswell’s own.

1738 <gai>nī bhus furachra do rēir ar n-inmhe:<eng> ‘more warily in our vocation’; cf. the positive <gai>fuireachair<eng> 1749. <gai>Inmhe<eng> also means “wealth, rank”, but here probably no more than “the state we are in” as revealed by the self-examination referred to in 1729-33. For another rendering of “vocation” cf. 1803.

1744 <gai>neoch agā bfuil a úidh:<eng> “who have his attention”; for <gai>neoch<eng> as a relative cf. note 63.

1746 <gai>is beite dhaoibh-se subhachas sīor-bhuan oraibh:<eng> ‘it ought greatly to rejoice you’. Cf. <gai>is beite dhaoibh-se fuireachair<eng> 1749 “you must be careful”, and <gai>nī h-ion-bhethe dhúinne uaimhneach<eng> CC 87 a 1 “we need not be afraid”, in both of which the subject of the finite verb becomes agent with the non-finite verb. It is interesting to note that when the subject is a noun and the complement a personal pronoun in the finite construction, as <gai>atá a fhios agaibh,<eng> then in the non-finite construction the complement becomes agent and the noun remains subject, e.g. <gai>is beite dhībh a dheirbh-fhios agaibh<eng> 2413, <gai>is beite dhaoibh a dheirbh-fhios agaibh<eng> 2478, not <gai>is beite dā fhios agaibh.<eng> Similarly here, and in cases like <gai>is ēidir lind a dheirbh-fhios do bheith againd<eng> 2204, <gai>rēr chóir dhúinn th’eagla do bheith oraind<eng> 2964 ‘whereby we have occasion to fear thee’, and <gai>bhus éidir leó náire dá ngabháil<eng> 3293 ‘they may be ashamed’.

1761 <gai>a lán-ndóchas do dhēnamh as fīrēntacht Iosa Crīosd:<eng> cf. <gai>ag dēnamh neamh-dhōchais as maitheas Dé<eng> 2190; as is properly a pronominal form, displacing the older a before a noun. Carswell has a in <gai>dōchas … a creatūiribh<eng> 630, and 3430-1 (where it is also found in CC 136 b), and a h- before a vowel in 165, 1359.

1773 At this point BCO inserts an exposition of the Creed.

1776 <gai>go madh feirrde:<eng> i.e. <gai>fearr<eng> + <gai>de,<eng> with the palatalisation extended to the -rr-; cf. note 1447.


[TD 148]

1777 <gai>roindfeam:<eng> ‘we shall divide’, which calls for the correction of <gai>roind,<eng> which may be a dittography from 1775.

1784 <gai>gurab é Dia …:<eng> “that he is our Father, Almighty, maker of heaven and earth”; the original has only commas where we have printed semicolons, and rather than assume that Carswell has confused the numbering of the items, we should perhaps admit the otherwise unlikely possibility that the numbers follow the items, and so punctuate—<gai>uile-chumhachtach, an dara ní; gurab é … talmhan é, an treas nī; goirmaoid ….<eng>

1796 <gai>nō comhfhurtacht crīochnuighe:<eng> ‘nor final joy’; on formal grounds <gai>críochnuighthe,<eng> more correctly so spelt, may be either participle or gen. of the verbal noun. There appear to be examples of <gai>comhfhurtacht<eng> in Carswell both as m., as here and 1929, and f. as 1597, but all depend on an absence of lenition which may be incorrect.

1803 <gai>macacht ochta:<eng> “adoption”, i.e. the state of being a <gai>mac ochta;<eng> cf. CC 46 b 2, and note 1648.

1804 <gai>ar nglōruighthe:<eng> ‘our glorification’; the original <gai>ar nglanta<eng> is a dittography from the preceding line.

1852 <gai>gan chion fir:<eng> Carswell’s addition “without the love of a man”; cf. <gai>gan chomand no chomhchaidreamh fir<eng> CC 50 b 6.

1863 <gai>agā bfuil cóir … ar ladh … do chuma … do rēir choinsiasa daonna:<eng> ‘that hath just authority and power to make laws to bind the consciences of men’; <gai>do rēir<eng> in a very general sense “with regard to”, as 1946.

1868 <gai>agas oibrighe sé so:<eng> ‘and this he doeth’; i.e. <gai>oibrighidh,<eng> but probably pronounced <gai>oibrí.<eng>

1877 <gai>gidh bé iarras … do-bheirid:<eng> ‘whosoever seeketh … they do not only blaspheme …’, with faulty concord in BCO also. Cf. <gai>féchadh … gach aon-duine … agas dā ndēnaid mur sin, ithid … agas ōlaid<eng> 2134, but <gai>gidh bē īosas no ōlfas … īosaidh agas ōlfaidh sé … do chiond nach dtug sé aire<eng> 2136.

1880 <gai>go fiú a gcumhachta:<eng> ‘as far as in them is’; cf. <gai>go feadh mo chumhachta<eng> 44, and DIL s.v. fíu 159.3.

1888 <gai>go h-aithghearr:<eng> ‘chiefly! Read <gai>go h-áraidhe<eng>?

1902 <gai>crochaidhe:<eng> ‘hanged’ or “hanging” here, ‘hanged on us’, i.e. “hung on us, attached to us” below 1907. The participle of <gai>crochadh<eng> is <gai>crochta;<eng> that of <gai>crochughadh<eng> would be <gai>crochaighthe,<eng> but no such verb seems to be recorded. Professor Thomson informs me


[TD 149]

that in his Lewis dialect the participle of <gai>crochadh<eng> is <gai>crochait(e),<eng> which recalls the normal Manx participle in -it; in that case, however, the earlier spellings show that -it has developed from -ighte, or -ichte, i.e. the suffix has been generalised from the verbs having Ir. -ughadh, Sc.G. -achadh in the verbal noun, but does not necessarily imply the previous existence of such verbs.

1904 <gai>mar bhrāighid orainde:<eng> ‘to be our pledge’; according to CIL s.v. brage the -a- is short, but Dinneen gives “braighe, also bráighe”. The long vowel occurs in CIL s.v. bráge “neck”, Dinneen brágha, and dat. pl. <gai>brāighdibh<eng> above 930. There seems to be some confusion between the two words, so that TSh glossary has brágha (a) “neck”, (b) “captive”, with -a- short in (b), but also in some of the (a) quotations. Through the sense of “captive” and “hostage” the meaning “security” develops.

1924 <gai>do-chuaidh sē suas … a bfiadhnuise tsúl daoine:<eng> ‘he visibly ascended to the heavens and was taken from the eyes of men’, where Carswell omits “and was taken from” and substitutes “in the presence of”.

1930 <gai>do fosgladh teaghduis Dē dhúinn:<eng> ‘the heavens are opened to us’; <gai>teaghdhuis<eng> “dwelling-place” here has the etymological spelling (cf. CDIL s.v. tegdais), but in the remaining occurrences -dh- is dropped, e.g. 2665, and, with a length-mark introduced, 3258.

1931 <gai>as a bfēdfam:<eng> apparently the relative form of the preposition as, “out of which”, referring to <gai>slighe,<eng> though there seems to be no ground for preferring <gai>as a<eng> to <gai>ina<eng> or <gai>ar a<eng> here. If an emendation is to be made, <gai>as go<eng> is the easiest.

1938 <gai>dār gcoimhéd agas dār gcoimh-dhīdean:<eng> ‘as well to instruct us as to comfort and maintain us’, where Carswell omits the first verb.

1964 <gai>nō lá an ath-nuadhaidh:<eng> an addition by Carswell. The reference is to Acts 3.19.

1978 <gai>arna gcumailt:<eng> ‘sprinkled’; cf. CC 90 b 3 and note 328 b 7. At 2612 below the meaning is “apply (a medicament)”.

1992 <gai>ré slāineōchar amhāin sind:<eng> ‘by whom only we are sanctified’, but Carswell has “shall be saved”.

1995 <gai>agas go mbia and Eagluis:<eng> ‘and shall be, even till the coming of the Lord Jesus, a Church’; Carswell omits the adverbial phrase.


[TD 150]

1997 <gai>ar son gur ghabh sī:<eng> ‘because it receiveth’, but Carswell has “has received”.

2011 <gai>uilidhe nó chatharrdha:<eng> ‘universal’; <gai>uilidhe<eng> has been used in this sense at 1996; <gai>catharrdha<eng> is the adjective from <gai>cathair<eng> “city”, whence the use of it to render bellum ciuile in the title of the text <gai>Cath Cathardha.<eng> Dwelly gives <gai>an creideamh catharra<eng> as “the Reformed (Protestant) religion”, but also <gai>an Eaglais ch.<eng> as “the Church militant,” where <gai>catharra<eng> seems to be associated with <gai>cath.<eng> The term “universal” was preferred by the reformers to “catholic” as restoring and clarifying the meaning of the latter. Despite the comprehensive definition in the following phrase, ‘because it consisteth and standeth of all tongues and nations; yea, of all estates and conditions of men and women’, the use of Book of the Universal Kirk as an alternative title for the record of the acts and proceedings of the General Assembly shows that it could also be applied to the Church as a whole within one state, in contrast to the individual congregation.

2031 <gai>macnas meanman:<eng> ‘an imagination’, i.e. “an illusion”, from <gai>macnas<eng> “sport, play”, and <gai>meanma<eng> “mind”; the phrase occurs again in CC 149 b 7, but CDIL s.v. macnas has no other examples.

2033 <gai>a chorp féin:<eng> ‘in his own body’; therefore probably for <gai>’na chorp.<eng> The reading <gai>a chorp<eng> may clarify <gai>én-chorp ar son chuirp eile,<eng> i.e. that the resurrected body is not a replacement of the earthly body by another, but in that case no question of deserving the same body can arise; the reference here is clearly to 2 Cor. 5.10 <gai>chum gu faigh gach neach na nithe a rinn e sa choluinn a réir an ni a rinn e, ma’s maith no olc e.<eng>

2038 <gai>biaid:<eng> this might be 3rd. per. pl. future, though the only example is <gai>beid<eng> 1234, and the pl. would be unusual even if Carswell had had <gai>gach uile ghlóir agas onóir<eng> like his original. The simplest correction is to <gai>biaidh,<eng> but to correspond to the English we require <gai>bīodh;<eng> cf. 2867 note.

2040 Here we return to the text of the Geneva Book.

2050 <gai>neoch atāmuid do bhaisdeadh:<eng> ‘whom we shall baptise’; similarly <gai>neoch atāmuid do thabhairt mar ofrāil<eng> 2094 ‘whom we offer’, which shows that do is not being used to convey futurity (like English “are to baptise”), but is equivalent to <gai>ag.<eng> Cf. notes 215, 849, 1024.


[TD 151]

2052 <gai>go n-aideōmhaidh sé:<eng> fut. of <gai>admháil;<eng> Carswell has -mh- here and in the verbal noun, otherwise -bh-.

2057 <gai>don bheatha-sa:<eng> cf. note 1286 for <gai>beatha<eng> as gen.; there seem to be no other examples of its use as dat. in place of the usual <gai>beathaidh.<eng>

2075 <gai>sinde do bheathughadh:<eng> ‘beautify and bless us’, but Carswell prefers the more mundane “nourish us”.

2076 <gai>agas:<eng> ‘but’, but Carswell does not follow the ‘not only … but also’ exactly, and has “inasmuch as thou art not content … with feeding us … and in addition … increasest in us … as our bounden duty we lift up…”. Cf. 2905-8.

2111 <gai>mar is in-dēnta:<eng> GB states that the monthly Communion is a common or general practice, but Carswell’s wording would make it mandatory. Calvin had wished for weekly Communion, but had been obliged to content himself with this monthly celebration, which accordingly appears here taken over from the Genevan practice. The Book of Discipline, however, has “four times in the year we think sufficient to the administration of the Lord’s Table” (Laing II 239), and this was formally enacted by the fifth General Assembly in December 1562 for the burghs, but “twice in the year in country parishes” (Calderwood II 209).

2117 1 Cor. 11. 23-29. The Irish Prayerbook version, f. Aa, the Epistle for Thursday before Easter, runs: Oír ios ón dtighearna fuáir meisi a ní thug mé dhíbhse mar an gcéudna, [eadhón] gur ghlac an tighearna Iosa arán sa noidhche ionar braitheadh hé: & ar mbreith bhuidheachais dó, do bhris sé [hé,] 7 a dubhairt sé, glacaidh, ithidh: a sé só mo chorpsa brisdear ar bhur sonsa: déunaidh so mar choimhniughadh oramsa. Mar an gcéudna fós [do ghlac sé] an copán, tar éis shuipéir, agá rádh, a sé an cupasa an tiomna nuá, trém fhuilse: déunaidh so dhá mhioncachd iobhtháoi [hé,] mar choimhniughadh oramsa. Oír ní fhuil dá mhionca iósas sibh an taránsa, 7 ióbhas sibh an cupasa, nách foillsigheann sibh bás an tighearna, gu teachd dó. Uime sin giodh bé iósas an taránsa, nó ióbhas cupá an tighearna gu neamhiomchuibhghe, biáidh sé cionntach dho chorp 7 dfhuil an tighearna. Ar a nadhbharsin dearbhadh gach áonduine hé féin, 7 mar sin itheadh sé dho naránsa, 7 ibheadh dhon chupasa. Oír giodh bé itheas 7 ibheas gu neamhiomchuibhghe, ithidh agus ibhidh sé do chum a dhamnuighthe féin, an tan nach déin eidirdhealughadh eidir chorp an tighearna [7 bhiádh eile.]


[TD 152]

2123 <gai>brisfidhear:<eng> ‘which is broken’, as <gai>brisdear<eng> in the Irish version above; Carswell uses the future as referring to events still to come when the words are spoken.

2128 <gai>īosas:<eng> also 2131, and in the Irish version above; the rel. s-future of <gai>ithe,<eng> 3rd. pers. sg. <gai>īosaidh<eng> 2137. Cf. GOI § 658 (a), CDIL s.v. ithid 326.46 and .73, and note in TSh glossary s.v. ithim the forms with -f- added to the íos- stem. Generally speaking, however, Carswell prefers <gai>caitheamh<eng> for “eat”, e.g. 2122, 2148, 2152-8, 2222, 3308.

2130 <gai>do bhreith bhreithe ar bheōuibh agas ar mharbhaibh:<eng> cf. the Creed 1154; Carswell adds these words to explain the purpose of the second coming.

2147 <gai>sul do rachas sé:<eng> cf. <gai>sul do sgrībhadh<eng> 65, <gai>sul sgaoilfeas siad<eng> 740; the former has do belonging to the verb, the latter shows the use of the relative after this conjunction. The other examples add only lenition of the verb as <gai>sol chaithid<eng> 3308. For <gai>sul<eng> cf. CC 162 b 7 note. In this instance do may go with the verb, i.e. do-rachas as fut. rel. of MIr do-tét “comes” (CDIL s.v. 379.16-30), or it may be an early instance of do, perhaps pronounced only as a, as a relative particle, as in the Shorter Catechism (CC xxxvii). Professor R. A. Breatnach refers (Eriu XVII 104) to the form sula, with -a possibly modelled on muna, or to be identified with the preposition i. In this particular example we could assume that Carswell said <gai>sula<eng> and wrote <gai>sul do<eng> as a false etymological spelling, but elsewhere he has only the classical <gai>sul.<eng>

2161 <gai>ag brosnughadh dhīoghaltais do dhēnamh oraind:<eng> ‘provoke him to plague us’, but Carswell has “provoking the inflicting of punishment upon us”.

2163-86 The passage here in the GB, the fencing of the Table, was taken, like the preceding part of this exhortation, from the Second Prayerbook of Edward VI; BCO rewrote this section, the first part of which was omitted from the English Prayer Book also at the revision of 1662. The opening section, 2142-62, reads as follows in the Irish Prayerbook, f. EEe 2 verso: A cháirde grádhacha san Tighearna, lé na mían teachd chum comháoineach naomhtha chuirp, agus fhola ar slanaightheóra Iósa Críosd ar dtighearna, is cóir dhaoibh a chuimhneachadh, an ní do sgríobh Pól apsdal chum na Gcóirintidhánach, 7 mar do bheir sé teagasg dhóibh, fá gach áon


[TD 153]

da dhearbhadh féin, sul do laimheóidís an taránso do ithe, agus an cupánso do ól. Oir amhuil is mór an tíodhlacadh, corp agus fuil Chríosd do chaitheamh go spioratálta, comhnuidhe iondainde, agus bheith a gcomháontachd leisean, (an ní theagmhus don druing tig go diongmhálta chum na comhaóineach, eadhon lé croidhe tuirseach umhal, maille ré creideamh fírindeach agus lé dóchus dearbhtha and sa trócuire do ghealladh dhuinn tré Chríosd:) Mar an gcéudna is mór an gabhadh dhuinn, da dtigeam chuigthe go neamhdhiongmhálta, do bhrídh go bfhuilmaóid cionntach a bfhuil, agus a bhféoil ar slánaighthéora Iósa Críosd, agus go gcaithmíd íad chum bhreitheamhnuis agus damanta, ar a nadhbhar nach déunmuid eidirdhealughadh eidir chorp an Tighearna, agus bhíadh oile. Agus tairngeadhmaóid a fheargsan agus a dhiomdha oruind, agus do bheirmíd fá deara dhó, dioghaltas do dhéunamh oruind, lé plághaibh agus ré gallraighthibh égsamhla, agus fá dhéoigh ris an mbhás.

The fencing, 2163-86, runs: Ar an adhbharsoin, má tá áonduine dhaóibhsi, na námhuid, agus na fhear sgandalach dho thabhairt dho bhréithir Dhé, na fhior adhaltrandais do dhéunamh, ar lasadh dhfeirg, ná dhfúath, nó cionntach a gcoir ar bioth oile, na lamhadh a iondshamhuilsin toigheachd chum búird an Tighearna: Achd sul thiocfas sé, cáoineadh sé a fpheacthaidh, ar eagla tar éis gabhála na comhaóineach náomhthasa dhó, go rachadh an Díabhal a naóinfheachd leis an gcomhaóineach and, mar do chuáidh sé ann Iudás, agus go líanfadh sé hé do nuile olc, agas go mbhéaradh leis hé chum damanta cuirp agus anma. Ar a nadhbharsoin a dhearbhraithreacha, beiridh féin bhreith nar naghuidh féin, deagla go mbhéuradh an Tighearna bhreith in bhar naghuidh. Déunuidh aithrighe fhírindeach sna peacthaibh dho chuáidh thoruibh: Cuiridh ar muinídhin, agus ar ndóchus do rún chroidhe and ar Slánaighthéoir, Iósa Críosd.

2176 <gai>gan a chroidhe do bheith ag én-duine … teacht:<eng> “that no one [of these we have mentioned] have the presumption to come”; a is probably, the reduced form of do in a partitive sense. The pejorative extension, whereby “heart” in the sense of “courage” passes over into “boldness, presumptuousness, obduracy” seems to be Sc.G. rather than Irish.

2185 <gai>agā bfuil súil ré:<eng> ‘who aspire to’; cf. <gai>ní fhuil mo tsúil ré slánughadh do thaobh ele<eng> 3316 “I hope for salvation from no other quarter”, and CDIL s.v. súil II for the sense “hope, expectation”.


[TD 154]

Dinneen treats this as a distinct word from <gai>súil<eng> “eye”, but this is unnecessary.

2207 <gai>lucht cathuighe:<eng> apparently “fighters”, possibly with reference to 2202, but in the context some such sense as “partakers” would be more appropriate (the English has no noun at all), and we might read <gai>lucht cuidighe<eng> as at 2220.

2210 <gai>’nar mbeathuigheadh:<eng> ‘in our lives’, but the Gaelic has a sg. noun, apparently not distinct from <gai>beathughadh,<eng> perhaps with the meaning “living, manner of life”; the gen. <gai>beathuighe<eng> 2212 also corresponds to ‘life’, perhaps with the sense “quickening, enlivening” rather than “feeding, nourishing”, though the latter is also appropriate in the context.

2214ff. In these lines I have followed the sentence division of 1567, as Carswell may have intended to change the construction; the English suggests rather that the first sentence should end with <gai>na h-Eagluise<eng> 2220, and that <gai>ōir asē sin … a fhola<eng> 2221-2 should be placed in brackets, so that <gai>nā fuilghemaoid<eng> is construed as an imperative, not, as the other punctuation implies, a noun clause explaining the <gai>caitheamh fīrindeach.<eng> This change, however, is incomplete unless we also add <gai>dhúinn<eng> after <gai>ar mbeith<eng> in 2220.

2222 <gai>nā fuilghemaoid:<eng> ‘let us not suffer’, or, in the original punctuation “that we do not allow”; we expect <gai>fuilngemaoid,<eng> but note in DIL s.v. fo-loing 277.12 the possibility of confusion with fo-luigi, which has MIr stem foilgh-.

2231 <gai>do ghabhāil na h-oileamhna saoire agas na beathadh subsdaintighe-se:<eng> ‘to receive nourishment, relief, and quickening of his substance’, but the Gaelic is “to receive this free nourishment and substantial life” (or possibly “food”, though ‘quickening’ favours the former); but cf. <gai>tug tú dhūinn fós mar oileamhain agas mar bheathaidh<eng> ‘and hast given him unto us as … food and nourishment’ 2314.

2235 <gai>a bflaitheamhnus Dé:<eng> ‘into heaven’; cf. <gai>a bflaitheamhnus<eng> 2059 “in heaven”, where the transition from “kingdom” via “kingdom of God” (as here) to “heaven” is complete, as it is in Manx flaunys, which has no other meaning.

2264 <gai>do sgrios an fhir dob adhbhar don bhás:<eng> ‘to destroy him that was author of death’; the English points to <gai>údhdar<eng> (as at 3493) as a likely correction of the original <gai>adhar,<eng> but the correction


[TD 155]

made is no less easy and “cause of death” would be a more intelligible phrase.

2270 <gai>a ghabhāil cuige:<eng> ‘to comprehend’; elsewhere in the text the forms of this pronominal preposition are permanently lenited so that <gai>cuige<eng> is probably only a misprint.

2274 <gai>an uair nārbh aithnidh dúinn:<eng> ‘when we could do nothing’; Carswell has already used <gai>aithnidh<eng> in the usual sense of “known” 356, 1419, but from here on he uses it several times as “possible”, e.g. <gai>gurab aithnidh dhuid féin gach én-ní ar a bfuil a fheidhm nō a fhurtacht do fhreasdal dó<eng> 2617 ‘and thou canst … minister unto him all such things as shall be necessary and expedient.’ Similarly 2668-81, 2975. This sense is not recognised in CDIL s.v. 3 aithnid, but the development from “known, familiar” is understandable. Cf. Des. 5894 for the phrase da ttuguinn dó an moladh as mó as aithnidh dhamh 7 as éidir leam, though it is by no means certain that <gai>aithnidh<eng> and <gai>éidir<eng> are meant to be synonymous.

2288 <gai>maille rē bfuil ar gcoinbhearsáid-ne go sdrāsda ar neamh:<eng> ‘with whom we have our conversation presently in heaven’; I supply <gai>coinbhearsáid<eng> from the Irish Prayerbook, f. Qq 2, the Epistle for Trinity XXIII: Achd as ar neamh atá ar gcoinbhearsáidne, an tionadh as a bhfuil súil aguinn fós ré teachd an tslanaighthóra, Philippians 3.20, one of the passages referred to in the marginal notes of GB. The word-division <gai>go sdrāsda<eng> follows the original edition, though the normal writing would be either in one word or trásda as the second; as <gai>trásda<eng> is an old metathesis of <gai>tráth-sa<eng> (before th ceased to be a voiceless dental spirant) the whole means “till this time, hitherto, already”.

2306 <gai>mar do bhādar na cédfadha:<eng> “as the senses were …”; the past tense is peculiar as the context is present. Carswell has shortened the original rubric here: ‘During the which time, some place of the Scriptures is read, which doth lively set forth the death of Christ, to the intent that our eyes and senses may not only be occupied in these outward signs of bread and wine, which are called the visible word, but that our hearts and minds also may be fully fixed in the contemplation of the Lord’s death, which is by this holy Sacrament represented.’

2314 <gai>mar bheathaidh in-mholta do chum na beathadh suthaine:<eng> ‘as a necessary … nourishment unto everlasting life’; but in-


[TD 156]

mholta, from <gai>moladh,<eng> is elsewhere “laudable, praiseworthy”, e.g. 684. A simple emendation would be <gai>in-mhelta<eng> from <gai>mealaim<eng> “use, consume, enjoy”, for which see CDIL s.v. meilid II, and Shorter Catechism (CC p. 233).

2333 The address to the reader, at the end of the Communion Order, in defence of the reformed manner of celebrating this sacrament, which was repeated in BCO from the Geneva Book, is omitted by Carswell.

2336 <gai>trí lá tsaoire:<eng> ‘three several days’, but <gai>lá saoire<eng> is really a day free from labour, a holy-day or holiday (cf. <gai>cuimhnidh lá na Sabbōide do chongbhāil a saoire<eng> 3444, <gai>’na lá saoire<eng> 3476); with the abolition of the feast-days the term became synonymous with Sunday. Cf. CDIL s.v. 1 saíre Ia and III.

2337-41 Carswell here includes a formula for proclaiming banns, which is not present in his original: “There is a contract or engagement of marriage between N. and N., and if there is anyone who has a proper and lawful reason that prevents or forbids it and shows that they cannot lawfully be married, let him now declare it.”

2343 The exhortation and the question put to the parties to be married (2406-17) are similar to passages in the First and Second Prayerbooks of Edward VI, but the exhortation is considerably expanded. With 2344-50 cf. Irish Prayer Book, f. NNn verso: A cháirde ró ghrádhacha, tangamair a gceand a chéile a bhfhíaghnaise Dé, agus do láithir a Eágailsi, chum na lánamhnachso do cheangal dá chéile andsa fphósadh náomhtha: Gárub sdaid onórach hé, dho orduigh Día a Bfháirchios, sul do fpheacthaibh [sic] Adhamh.

2354 <gai>ōs ciond ēisg na fairrge:<eng> ‘over … the fishes of the sea’, so that we might expect <gai>iasg;<eng> but iasg is collective as well as sg., as is exemplified in the Sc.G. rendering of Psalm 8.8, where the same idea is expressed, as <gai>eunlaith nan speur agus iasg na mara<eng> (where the Modern Irish version has éisc na farraige). Cf., however, <gai>mar tugais … ēisg na fairrge<eng> 3852.

2356 <gai>’na uathadh nō ’na ēnar:<eng> ‘alone’; for this use of <gai>uathadh<eng> “small number” see CDIL s.v. úathad 42.39.

2406 Cf. Irish Prayer Book, f. NNn 2: Aithnidhim dhíbh go ró dhíthcheallach (amhail do bhéurthaói cúndas uáibh a lo bháoghlach an bhreitheamhnuis, iona bhfhoillséochar rún na nuile chroídheadh) madh feasach do cheachdar dhíbh toirmiosg ar bioth as nách féidir dhaóibh bheith ceangailte dhá chéile go dlisteanach a bphósadh, a


[TD 157]

fhoillsiughadh anois. Oír bíoth a dhearbh so agaibh, an mhéide cheangaltar san fphósadh, achd do réir bhréithre Dé, nách hé Día cheanglas íad, agus nách fuil a bphósadh dlisdeanach. Carswell expands the first clause ‘I require and charge you’, by “requiring” the one party to the marriage and “charging” the other.

2415 <gai>bhur gceangal … nach bfuiltí ceangailte:<eng> the introduction of the 2nd pers. is Carswell’s.

2421 <gai>nach gcluinim … ar pósadh do dhēnamh:<eng> added by Carswell to explain what the congregation is being invited to bear witness to.

2464 Matthew 19. 3-6.

2470 <gai>sgaraidh duine rēna athair agas rēna mhāthair:<eng> ‘a man shall leave father and mother’; cf. 2363 where the same phrase renders ‘leaveth his father and mother’. “Shall” here is the mark of custom or obligation rather than of futurity, and the present is a perfectly admissible rendering. This passage is quoted in ID 131 as an example of the Sc.G. use of present for future, but there are better examples in Carswell, for which see the Introduction.

2497 The visitation of the sick in GB is confined to the general directions in 2500-18; the remainder is added in BCO.

2511 <gai>dá rabh uireasbhuidh riachtanais a leas ar an easlān:<eng> ‘if he perceive him to want any necessaries’; cf. note 120. The phrase is used here in the concrete sense of “something needed”, for which see CDIL s.v. riachtanas (b); for the abstract sense, “need, lack”, cf. 3079.

2512 <gai>cungnamh leis:<eng> ‘he … relieveth him’; so spelt at 3003, but also <gai>congnamh<eng> 2712. In GB 2500-15 is all one sentence, though with a capital letter at 2510, and there is in the Gaelic a series of non-finite verbs, <gai>teagasg do thabhairt<eng> 2503, <gai>bagar do dhēnamh<eng> 2507, now <gai>cungnamh,<eng> and <gai>tabhairt<eng> 2513, all depending on <gai>lēigmaoid … do chum tuigse an Mhinistir<eng> 2502. The connection is nearly lost in the Gaelic and a 3rd. sg. imperative would have been clearer. It is, of course, possible that cungnamh is intended as an imperative; cf. note 2744.

2513 <gai>ar feadh a nearta nó a chumhachta:<eng> ‘according to his ability’; <gai>ar feadh<eng> is usually “throughout” and <gai>go feadh,<eng> “to the limit of” as at 44, 3484. There is no evidence for <gai>neart<eng> as a u-stem, so that <gai>nearta<eng> (also at 3484) cannot be its gen. sg., for which Carswell has <gai>neirt.<eng> It is probably the gen. sg. of <gai>neartadh<eng> “strengthen-


[TD 158]

ing”, which is occasionally used as a synonym of neart. Cf. gen. <gai>foirrnearta<eng> 1062.

2515 <gai>dlighidh:<eng> ‘may’, i.e. “is entitled to”, the less common sense.

2516 <gai>neoch do-ní guidhe … mar an gcēdna:<eng> ‘who doth not only make prayers for him there presently, but also, if it so require, commendeth him in the public prayers to [of?] the congregation.’

2535 <gai>gallradh:<eng> ‘sickness’, but probably gen. pl. like the parallel <gai>easslāinteadh;<eng> hardly from <gai>galar,<eng> but probably an instance of <gai>galradh,<eng> of which CDIL G 41.3 records only one example.

2538 <gai>ag tabhairt tséla ar ndroch-bheatha dhúinn:<eng> ‘advertising us of our evil life’, i.e. “warning us”; <gai>séla<eng> elsewhere is “seal”, here “a clear, authentic sign”, almost “a proof”.

2569 <gai>indsuidhe imarcacha:<eng> ‘assaults’; historically spelt <gai>indsaighthe.<eng>

2593 <gai>atá tú anois agā fhios:<eng> ‘thou now visitest him’; for this sense of <gai>fios,<eng> parallel to <gai>fiosrughadh<eng> 2497, 2519, see DIL s.v. 1 fis I (c).

2596 <gai>gé nach bfuil a theangaidh … aibēil:<eng> ‘although the tongue be not able’; cf. CDIL s.v. aipéil “quick, sudden”, while the derivatives aipéile “quickness, flattery”, and aipélugud “adulation”, are still further removed from the sense required here. The other examples in Carswell are <gai>atá do ghnáth ullamh ésgaidh aibēil<eng> 2765 ‘ever in readiness’, and <gai>lé bfétar misi do dhenamh aibél do ghabháil thoraidh<eng> 3153 ‘the which may make me able so to profit’; that at 2765 may very well be taken as “swift”, but the other two are more naturally understood as “able”. CDIL 162.65 says “with influence of English able”, assuming the Irish word has acquired a modified meaning from a similar English word; it is equally probable that English able was already a current borrowing which Carswell identified in form with a native word. Early Manx had similarly borrowed it as abyl, and Irish as ábalta, with adaptation to the suffix of spesialta and the like.

2598 <gai>do fhoillseochadh:<eng> formally the conditional, as at 1495, and so with <gai>glóir<eng> acc. sg., but BCO’s ‘to set forth thy glory’ suggests it should be the verbal noun and that accordingly we require <gai>glóire.<eng> None of CDIL’s quotations under oific gives the construction which follows, but we expect either the gen. of the verbal noun (excluded here because of <gai>a oifige<eng> “its office”), or do with the verbal noun, as in Des. 2520-22, 3011-12.


[TD 159]

2599 <gai>ar a tson sin:<eng> “despite that, nevertheless”, answering to <gai>gé nach bfuil<eng> 2596. So perhaps at 1734, and cf. CDIL s.v. 1 son II (f).

2605 <gai>is ēidir lé nāmhaid ar n-anma … ar gcoinsiasa:<eng> ‘which the enemy of our salvation may raise up to trouble his conscience’, but Carswell uses the 1st. pers. pl. throughout.

2623 <gai>a thriall agas a tslighe:<eng> ‘his recourse and access’; perhaps simply “course and path”, but cf. CDIL s.v. tríall (b) “endeavour, purpose”, and especially the last quotation, do eisd in Tigherna trial ocus iniaratus na mbocht from Smaointe B. Chr. 2610, rendering desiderium pauperum exaudiuit Dominus (Psalm 10.17). See note 2811.

2637 <gai>dó ina uile pheacthuibh … go bfuighe sé maitheamh …:<eng> ‘to all sinners, to obtain remission of all their sins’; Carswell here (cf. 2605) applies the general statement particularly to the sick man. He also adds an explanatory <gai>do bhrīdh a dhóchais as<eng> 2640 “because of his confidence in him”.

2645 <gai>tasdáil nua agas fēchain:<eng> ‘a new taste of; the original print has the unique <gai>sasdáil,<eng> and, although in its lenited forms this would not be distinct from the loan-word tástáil (CDIL s.v.), for which there is only one example recorded, the correction seems certain, not least because foreign verbs tend to be assigned the -áil suffix.

2657 <gai>creidmhe:<eng> an isolated form of the gen. of <gai>creideamh,<eng> for which Carswell normally has <gai>creidimh,<eng> as do CC and TSh. For this form cf. Des. 3423, 4641, where it is also the less frequent alternative.

2701 <gai>mur dhearrna tú … mun tuga tú …:<eng> both <gai>mur<eng> and <gai>mun<eng> are “unless”, and are paralleled by the longer forms <gai>mura, mara<eng> and <gai>muna<eng> (cf. 237 note, only muna in CC). Cf. R. A. Breatnach, Eriu XVII 104 “mara …, which developed in the late modern period from muna [in the footnote he quotes seventeenth-century examples], in all probability owes its form to the influence of <gai>gurab<eng> and <gai>nárab<eng> with which <gai>munab<eng> was associated in the subjunctive.”

2729 <gai>tar éis ar dtrēigthe féin dúin:<eng> ‘forsaking ourselves’; the verbal noun is elsewhere <gai>trēigean,<eng> but this form of the gen. sg. goes rather with <gai>tréigeadh,<eng> cf. CDIL s.v. trécud. No gen. forms seem to be recorded but cf. the verbal of necessity tréigthe in TSh 2285.

2737 From GB again.

2741 <gai>gan ní ar bith eile do phrósession nó dh’ordughadh:<eng> ‘without any further ceremonies’; this meagre provision for funerals is


[TD 160]

explained in the Book of Discipline (Laing II 250): “for albeit thingis song and red may admonishe some of the levying to prepair thame selfis for death, yit shall some superstitious and ignorant personis ever think, that the workis synging or reading of the levyng do and may proffat the dead.” And ibid. p. 251: “We are nott ignorant that some requyre ane Sermone at the Buriall, or ellis some places of Scripturis to be red, to put the leving in mynd that thei ar mortall, and that lykwyse thei must dye.” If this demand were granted “eather shall the Ministeris for the most parte be occupyed in preaching Funerall sermonis, or ellis thei shall have respect to personis, preaching at the buriall of the rich and honorabill, but keaping silence when the poore or dispysed departeth; and this with saif conscience can not the Ministeris do.” Carswell’s omission of the BCO’s words ‘if he [the minister] be present and required’ may be an attempt to provide that there should always be at least ‘some comfortable exhortation’ at funerals, but he may merely have been following GB at this point.

2744 <gai>dēnadh:<eng> “let him make”. The original has <gai>denamh<eng> and similarly <gai>do dhenamh<eng> for <gai>do-dhēnadh,<eng> 2842; denamh is so common that the compositor may have set it automatically, but it may also point to the phonetic identity of the two final syllables. This is better evidence that Carswell pronounced -adh in the same way as -amh (see ID 69) than his spellings <gai>futhaibh<eng> beside <gai>futhadh<eng> (cf. notes 1097, 1277), where another explanation is possible.

2754 <gai>óir is gloine í … uirre:<eng> ‘which requireth more purely to be governed’, whereas Carswell has “for it is purer and more proper to be governed”.

2768 <gai>agā Eagluis, lé ndēnaid foghlaim…:<eng> ‘left by God unto his church, whereby men learn …’; the transition from sg. <gai>eagluis<eng> to the 3 pl. <gai>dēnaid<eng> seems rather harsher than is usual in this constructio ad sensum, and it may be that we should follow GB more closely and insert <gai>daoine.<eng> Cf., however, <gai>don Eagluis … gurab éigean dōibh … do ghabhāil chuca<eng> 2846-9.

2776 <gai>áirfidhe:<eng> see note 1332.

2779 <gai>tearmond:<eng> for the shift in meaning whereby Latin terminus came to be equivalent to “sanctuary” in Irish, cf. CDIL s.v. termonn.

2801 <gai>rún ro-leasaighthe:<eng> “intention of improvement”; ro- seems


[TD 161]

to be purely for the sake of alliteration and not to have any intensive force. Similarly <gai>lucht luath-bhrisde th’aitheantadh<eng> 2999.

2802 <gai>a bfiadhnuise deise nō trír d’fhiadhnuise maith:<eng> ‘in presence of two or three witnesses’; Carswell’s final phrase is perhaps predicative “by way of reliable testimony”. The absence of lenition in <gai>maith<eng> must be erroneous, for <gai>fiadhnuise<eng> even in the sense of “a witness” is f.

2811 <gai>ó tslighidh agas ó thoil mhaith:<eng> ‘of a godly zeal’, where <gai>slighe<eng> must presumably mean “purpose, intention”, as the parallel <gai>triall<eng> seems to do at 2623.

2836 <gai>fear ghabhāla ceandaigh a n-adhaidh na córa:<eng> “briber”, which now suggests the offerer rather than the taker, but in NED s.v. Briber sense 3 “a taker of bribes” has quotations from 1520 to 1611, while sense 4 “a giver of bribes” is first attested in 1583.

2839 <gai>úsuruidhe:<eng> ‘usurer’; not in this form in CDIL, but cf. <gai>úsaire<eng> “usurer”, <gai>úsaracht<eng> “usury”, <gai>úsúrach<eng> “usurious”.

2840 <gai>lucht eiritice nó saobh-chreidimh nó droch-tsligheadh:<eng> ‘any heresy or sect’; <gai>eiritice<eng> is presumably gen. sg. of a noun “heresy”, but DIL has only eirice “heretic”, eiricecht and eiriticecht “heresy”, and eretic “heretic” and eretecda, -dach “heretical”. For <gai>droch-tslighe,<eng> cf. <gai>slighe<eng> 2941 and CDIL s.v. slige II (e), where this sense is supported by a single quotation. The origin of this use of the word may be found in Acts 9.2 <gai>nam faigheadh e aon neach do’n t-slighe so,<eng> and again in chs. 19 and 24.

2867 This sentence, Matthew 18.17, is from BCO: <gai>biaidh<eng> renders let him be’ (cf. note 2038 for another case where <gai>bíodh<eng> would be a more exact translation). This passage provided DIL with its only example of <gai>éticeach,<eng> and it is possible that we should read <gai>étniceach,<eng> a borrowing of ethnicus; cf. DIL s.v. etnic, and NED s.v. ethnic (B) n. “a Gentile, heathen, pagan”, recorded from c. 1375 to 1728.

2870 The next item after the Order of Ecclesiastical Discipline in BCO is the metrical psalter, then Calvin’s catechism, then his short catechism (which is the basis of Carswell’s), and finally various domestic prayers and graces. At this date the Gaelic translations of the Psalms (1657, 1694) and the Catechism (1631) still lay in the future, and Carswell, following GB, and passing over these two, comes next to the domestic prayers.

2880 <gai>do-nīmaoid ort:<eng> ‘which we shall make to thee’; the 1st.


[TD 162]

pl. fut. would probably be written <gai>do-dhénam,<eng> but as the sense is iterative the present is equally appropriate.

2891 <gai>an ní lēr thuilleamar:<eng> ‘whereby we deserve’, in which Carswell quite legitimately uses the perfect; <gai>an ní<eng> is presumably meant to clarify the antecedent, and would be appropriate if it had been a clause, but rather obscures the connection with <gai>ar bpeacaidh.<eng>

2899 <gai>agas gach én-lá eile feadh ar mbeathadh:<eng> “and every other day throughout our life”; added by Carswell, who elsewhere has <gai>ar feadh.<eng>

2912 <gai>ar gcroidheadha d’fhoillsiudhadh:<eng> ‘to lighten our hearts’; see note 1402.

2953 <gai>do mhēdughadh a dtīrēnacht agas a bfoirrnearta:<eng> ‘to increase their tyranny’; <gai>tīrēnacht<eng> has escaped the notice of CDIL, though tíránach (and one example of tírēnach) and tíránta are included.

2956-69 At this point a passage in GB appropriate to the exiled condition of its first users is replaced in BCO by the passage which Carswell translates.

2977 <gai>go h-umhal ómanta:<eng> ‘humbly’; <gai>ómanta<eng> (also at 3172) is not noted in CDIL and there can hardly be any connection with its noun omántas. Cf. probably omoint ibid. 142.50, if the meaning of the latter were more certain.

2987 In GB and BCO the next item is a prayer before meals, but Carswell has postponed the graces and proceeds next to an evening prayer. See below 3804 ff.

3025 <gai>agas go madh móide, a Thighearna, bhus toil leat sin d’admhāil, a fhaigsin duid …:<eng> ‘and so much the rather, O Lord, because …’; the words <gai>bhus toil leat sin d’admhāil<eng> “may it please thee to admit it” are Carswell’s own, but “so much the more” refers back to “weigh” (<gai>go mbreathnōchamaois<eng> 3023) and “repent” (<gai>go ndēnmaois aithrighe<eng> 3024), i.e. we are to consider and repent of our sins all the more seriously when we consider the fate of those who fail to do so. Carswell’s rendering means something quite different. The construction in <gai>a fhaigsin duid<eng> (which, in line with the thought of the English, should rather be <gai>dúinn<eng>) is abnormal; cf. note 919.

3051 <gai>aislinge nó taibhsidhe nó sbiorada buaidheartha:<eng> this prayer was evidently translated from BCO rather than GB, as the former has here ‘fantasies, dreams, or other temptations’, while GB has ‘temptations, fantasies, dreams, or illusions’.


[TD 163]

3057 <gai>as go madh ullmhuide … sin:<eng> “that we may be the more ready to …”; cf. note 1447.

3059 <gai>a n-āmhaillibh agas a dtrēighibh diadha:<eng> ‘in all godly conversation’, i.e. “behaviour, manner of life”; for <gai>tréidhe,<eng> correctly so spelt, see CDIL s.v. tréide I “set of three, triad”, and then, apparently because of triadic enumerations of good qualities, II “accomplishments, characteristics, qualities”.

3063 ‘A godly prayer to be said at all times’ from BCO (Laing VI 357).

3065 <gai>adhmoladh:<eng> printed ádh-, and similarly ágh- 3653, 3860; for the lengthening see Celtica I 340.

3082 <gai>ar son ar bfulaing a bfad d’aimsir:<eng> ‘for sparing us so long’.

3083 <gai>ag tabhairt aimsire faide:<eng> ‘giving so large a time’; <gai>fada<eng> here forms a gen. f. sg. <gai>faide<eng> like the comparative.

3100 <gai>mēdaidh … agas cuir a leithead do Rīghacht:<eng> ‘amplify and increase thy Kingdom’.

3116 <gai>a n-umhlu agas a n-onōrudh th’anma naomhtha-sa:<eng> ‘in the sanctifying and honour of thine holy Name’; the Gaelic is rather “in the obedience and honouring …”, assuming that <gai>umhlu<eng> is for <gai>umhla. Onōrudh<eng> is probably <gai>onóradh,<eng> cf. <gai>frithōlugh<eng> 3358, and CDIL s.v. onóraid. O’Rahilly notes (ID 67-8) that -ughadh has been for at least three centuries reduced to -ú in Irish, but it would be contrary to the whole character of Carswell’s spelling to assume that he intended <gai>umhlú<eng> and <gai>onórú<eng> here.

3126 The concluding petition, ‘O Lord, increase our faith’, is omitted by Carswell.

3127 ‘A prayer to be said of the child before he study his lesson.’ This follows directly on the preceding in BCO.

3130 Psalm 119.9, 18, and 34.

3146 <gai>lé toil thindeasnaidh:<eng> cf. <gai>tindeanas<eng> “haste” 1676, from which this adjective is derived. CDIL s.v. tinnesnach gives “hurried, urgent, violent, sudden”, but Carswell is using it rather for “eager”.

3147 <gai>iondas nach rachadh mo tsaothar a ndolaidh oram:<eng> “lest my labour should be wasted (to my loss)”; for <gai>dolaidh<eng> see CDIL s.v. dolud, the antonym of <gai>soladh, solaidh<eng> “good fortune”. The expression <gai>dul a ndolaidh<eng> ‘be wasted, come to naught’ is not recorded. Cf. <gai>dul


[TD 164]

a ndīomhaoineas<eng> 3155 with similar meaning, and, with a different preposition, Sc. G. <gai>dol a dholaidh.<eng>

3150 <gai>oraind:<eng> for <gai>oram;<eng> the pl. is not appropriate to the speaker here, nor is this a congregational prayer.

3156 <gai>ge bé h-aigneadh ar a dtiubhra mé aire:<eng> ‘to what study soever I apply myself’, i.e. “to whatever subject I give my attention”; this is a rare sense of <gai>aigneadh,<eng> for which cf. CDIL s.v. 1 aicned (e) “knowledge, science”. Des. 6449 may have something of the same sense, but the context there is not very explicit.

3180 <gai>Na tiodhluice …:<eng> added by Carswell; “these gifts and every other good gift I ask of thee, O merciful Father, in the name of thy Son Jesus Christ, as he himself has taught me saying, Our Father, etc.”

3185 Psalm 25.14. <gai>Foillseōchaidh … do-bhēra …:<eng> both future, though the English has present tenses.

3188 ‘A prayer to be said before a man begin his work.’

3191 <gai>aithne:<eng> probably for <gai>a aithne<eng> as at 1583, 2920, where a noun clause follows to which a refers.

3192 <gai>atāmaoid ag eadar-ghuidhe do ghrás:<eng> but ‘we beseech thee of thy grace’.

3194 <gai>as go soitheōchadh an beandachadh-sin:<eng> ‘that thy blessing may extend’; the -th- spelling is also found in <gai>go soithe<eng> “until” 1410, 1924, literally “until thou reach”, 2nd. sg. pres. subj. of 1 saigid (cf CDIL s.v.), for -ch-, and in a number of other words, for which see note 767. The basis for an -eó- conditional in this verb is obscure to me; emendation to <gai>bfaideóchadh<eng> (cf. CC 217 b 5.2) would be drastic.

3229 At this point BCO ends. The following prayer comes immediately after the evening prayer in both GB, where it is the last item, and in BCO. It was originally entitled ‘A prayer made at the first assembly of the congregation, when the confession of our faith and whole orders of the church was there read and approved’.

3271 <gai>d’iumchor chroiche éigne:<eng> ‘to suffer some cross’; for <gai>iumchur<eng> cf. note 1713. For <gai>éigin<eng> “some” cf. DIL s.v. éicin II, originally the dat sg. of éicen (= éigean), and so invariable. Carswell here treats it as an ordinary adjective and forms a regular gen. f. sg.

3277 <gai>ina ghlōir:<eng> “in his glory” placed by Carswell as if to refer


[TD 165]

to Christ’s second coming in glory, while GB has ‘that in his glory we may also be like him when he shall appear’.

3284 <gai>a thoile:<eng> but ‘thy will’ in GB.

3305 Carswell has now concluded his translation of GB and BCO proper (excluding the graces, which will appear later), and now reverts to the catechism, substituting an expanded version of Calvin’s little catechism, “The manner to examine children before they be admitted to the Supper of the Lord”, for the forbidding bulk of his large catechism (i.e. CC). The portions taken over more or less exactly from Calvin are 3312-3321, 3364-3381, 3389-3395, 3588-3614 (except 3593-8), 3628-30, 3701-4, 3713.

3312 <gai>An Maighistir:<eng> but ‘The Minister’; and 3314 <gai>An Descibul,<eng> but ‘The Child’.

3337 <gai>Ciondas ele:<eng> not “how else”, but “how, then”; cf. <gai>créd eile<eng> 3352 “what, then”, and 3413, 3505, but “what else” at 3385.

3379 <gai>ōna peacaibh:<eng> ‘from their sins’, and so probably to be corrected to <gai>bpeacaibh;<eng> but the strict concord with <gai>Eagluis<eng> is also possible, and is therefore retained in the text, though the pl. is evidenced elsewhere, as 2768 note.

3430 For other versions of the Commandments cf. CC 217 b, taken from the Irish Prayer Book, f. LLL 2 verso (also at CCc 2 verso), and Shorter Catechism (CC p. 248). The Shorter Catechism version clearly owes much to Carswell’s, the only differences between them being the result of slight modernisation of some expressions: thus <gai>agā mbia m’fhuath<eng> 3439 and <gai>agā mbia mo ghrádh<eng> 3440 become <gai>fhuathuigheas me<eng> and <gai>ghradhuigheas me,<eng> and the future <gai>fhoillseōchas<eng> 3439 becomes <gai>fhoillsidheas; do chongbhāil a saoire<eng> 3444 (see note 2336) becomes <gai>a chonbhail no a choimhed naomhtha;<eng> the sequence of imperatives in 3447-8 is simplified; <gai>an taisteallach bhias taoibh astoigh dod t’árus<eng> 3449 becomes <gai>en duine coimheach ata don taobh a stoigh dod dhoirsibh; gach ní dhā bfuil inta sin<eng> 3452 becomes <gai>gach ni ata ionta; an Sathurn<eng> (see note below) 3454 becomes <gai>an Tsaboid; iondas go<eng> 3455 becomes <gai>chum go<eng> (which Carswell also uses), and the future <gai>do-bhéra<eng> 3456 becomes <gai>do bheir; dēna<eng> 3457-8 becomes <gai>den; adhaltrus<eng> 3457 becomes <gai>adhaltrannus<eng> (which Carswell also uses); the regular neutral gen. sg. of the consonant stem <gai>comharsand<eng> 3459-60 gives way to a palatal <gai>comharsoin,<eng> -ain, while the palatal dat. sg. <gai>cumhursaind<eng> 3462 becomes neutral <gai>comharsan; a bhó<eng> 3461 becomes <gai>a dhamh.<eng> Apart from the replacement of non-popular


[TD 166]

words and forms, a few changes are made to bring the text into line with the AV. The version in CC 217 b from the Catechism in the Irish Prayer Book is an almost perfect copy of the Irish text, except for the first and second commandments, the area covered by the cancel, where there is a verbatim but not a literatim correspondence. The Irish text of this passage reads: 1. Ná bíoth áon ndía oile agad, achd meisi amháin. 2. Ná déuna dhuit féin Iomhágh, na cosmhuile aóinneith da bhfhuil thúas ar neamh, ná ar talmhain thíos, ná and sa nuisge fá thalmhuin: Ná cróm thú féin síos dóibh, agus ná tabhair onóir dhóibh. Oír is meisi do Thighearna Dia, agus is Día éudmhar mé, leanas peacthadh na naithreadh ar an gcloind go soithche an treas agus an ceathramhadh geinelach don drúing fhuaithidheas mé, agus do ní trócuire ar na míltibh don druing ghrádhaigheas mé, agas choimhéudas mhaitheanta.

3432 <gai>íomhaigh ghrafinte:<eng> “graven image”; cf. SC (CC, p. 248) <gai>iomhaigh ghraifne,<eng> CC 143 b 1 <gai>grafaint,<eng> and the verbal noun <gai>grafadh<eng> 339 a 5, parallel to <gai>cur a gclo<eng> (though it is also used as a loan form of English graf(t); see CC 184 b 2 note). The Irish versions do not translate “graven” at all, and the expression renders a single Hebrew word. Though CDIL has several similar terms for “writing, sketching, grubbing up”, there are no exact parallels to any of these. The Irish OT uses grabhálta, and later greannta.

3446 <gai>Sathurn:<eng> and also at 3454. The Jewish Sabbath, of course, falls on Saturday, so that Carswell is historically correct in using <gai>Sabbōid<eng> and <gai>Sathurn<eng> as synonyms. The reflexes of sabbatum or dies sabbati remain the ordinary Romance terms for “Saturday”, but it is dies Saturni that provides the basis of the Celtic and English names. Carswell was obviously unaffected by the tendency to identify the Sabbath of the old dispensation with the Sunday or Lord’s Day of the new, a tendency which became common only in the seventeenth century. Cf. below 3548-60.

3461 <gai>a bhó:<eng> now “his ox”, but the Hebrew word, normally “ox”, is twice found in a context where it must refer to a female and accordingly is translated “cow”.

3466 <gai>roindfead:<eng> “I shall divide”; the only instance in Carswell of the inflected 1st. sg. fut.

3473 <gai>gan deilbh do dhēnamh:<eng> “not to make any image”; cf. Des. p. 272, where examples of both nom. and acc. after gan in this construction are collected. <gai>Deilbh<eng> is properly speaking acc. or dat.


[TD 167]

sg. of <gai>dealbh,<eng> (gen. sg. <gai>deilbhe<eng> 1065), but at 3511 we have <gai>deilbh fhīrindeach<eng> as nom. sg. Since <gai>dealbh<eng> is not attested at all in Carswell we may assume that he uses only deilbh as nom., as below 3491, 3513.

3495 <gai>agas gur ghnáthach:<eng> cf. <gai>agas gurab suarraighe iad<eng> 3534 for <gai>go<eng> introducing a formally subordinate clause which has on main clause on which to depend. The meaning of <gai>agas go<eng> in both cases is “seeing that”, as with the formally non-subordinate <gai>agas é a n-aitheantaibh Dé<eng> 3516 and similarly 3606.

3539 <gai>luigheas lámha nó uaisle nó cumhachta rīogh nó tighearna:<eng> note the direct objects after <gai>luighe,<eng> instead of the prepositions <gai>tar<eng> and <gai>fo.<eng> Similarly Dinneen has <gai>mionnuighim an bhacall<eng> “I swear by the crozier”.

3558 <gai>’na suaine suthain:<eng> “as a perpetual …”; suaine is obscure. M’L took it as from <gai>suan<eng> and so translated “rest”; Professor Matheson was considering a connection with <gai>suaitheantus<eng> or the like and proposed the translation “symbol”. In that case the spelling should rather be <gai>suaithne,<eng> from <gai>so<eng> + <gai>aithne<eng> “a thing easily recognised”, with the general meaning “sign”.

3577 <gai>orraim:<eng> “respect”; see CDIL s.v. airraim, with variants uirrim (so TSh), oirrim, urraim. Similarly paired with <gai>onóir<eng> 3802.

3650 <gai>bhéras tú:<eng> “will you give” makes good sense, though “do you give” is perhaps better. The form is irregular, combining as it does the sense “give” with the absence of do-, and -é- of the future with the relative ending -as (cf. <gai>do-bhéra<eng> “which he shall give” 3456).

3656 <gai>do dhórtadh orra:<eng> we should expect either <gai>mar dob áil lé Dia … orra<eng> “as it has pleased God to pour out his graces upon them”, or possibly the present or future (<gai>is áil, bhus áil<eng>), followed by <gai>oram<eng> “upon me”. Carswell’s expression is, however, defensible if “saints” is to be understood in the NT sense, but the context makes this unlikely.

3675 <gai>Caidh é:<eng> similarly 3705, and Des. caidhe, caidhi, but written as one word and without length-mark. Meyer tentatively suggested OIr cote, pl. coteet, as the origin, for which see GOI § 462, but there is a discrepancy between OIr -t- (= d) and MIr -d- (= dh). Dr E. G. Quin discusses the problems in Eriu xx 140-150, though without including these examples. He accepts OIr cote as the origin of both <gai>caidhe,<eng> stressed on the first syllable, and modern Sc.G. <gai>gu dé,<eng> and suggests “a dialectal split going a long way back in the history


[TD 168]

of the language”. Carswell’s accent is not likely to be misplaced (the main group of occurrences of that lies between 336 and 893), but seems to provide the only evidence for the coincidence of -dh- and final stress.

3684 <gai>sa chánoin ina dtuigmaoid féin é:<eng> similarly <gai>’na gcanóin choimhidhigh nach dtuigeand an pobul … acht sa chanóin choitchind thuigfeas an coimthionōl<eng> 3754-6. M’L translated <gai>canóin<eng> as “language” and this is supported by the reference in all three places to understanding; the form of the word corresponds to that used for “canon”. If the latter were correct “canon” would have to have the generalised sense of “prescribed method” of prayer or administering the sacraments, and would imply that not only the language but the form of the ceremonies obscured their meaning and purpose. While the reformers would probably have agreed with this, though regarding it as an understatement, the assumption that <gai>canóin<eng> is a vernacular variant of <gai>canamhain<eng> (cf. note 230), in line with the generally simpler language of the catechism, remains more probable.

3687 <gai>Scriphtūir:<eng> an isolated spelling, probably to be corrected to <gai>Scribhtūir.<eng>

3733 <gai>briathra Dé do tsearmóin:<eng> and similarly <gai>lē searmōin bhriathar nDé<eng> 3734; for other examples of <gai>searmóin<eng> as a verbal noun cf. CDIL s.v. sermóin 192.60.

3741 <gai>rún umhla … aca:<eng> although the noun <gai>umhla<eng> seems possible the adjective <gai>umhal<eng> would be better here, “and having a humble desire (or, intention) of having him baptised”.

3771 <gai>na suibhiscēul:<eng> “of the evangelists”; cf. CDIL s.v. suibiscél, and CC <gai>suighscel<eng> 368 b 4 (also gen. pl.).

3779 <gai>agas:<eng> perhaps better <gai>acht,<eng> as it stands between one point of difference and one of similarity.

3786-9 <gai>Ní fhuil sé mar sin and … acht gé tá sé go spioratālta lēna chumhachtaibh:<eng> “He is not so present … although he is spiritually [present] by his power”; the second “present” may be understood from the first, or <gai>and sin<eng> or <gai>do láthair<eng> may be supplied after sé.

3804 Carswell now returns to the graces which he omitted between the morning and evening domestic prayers. The first is from GB, the second mixed, the remainder from BCO.

3805 Psalm 104.27, 28.

3805-15 A modification of the first part of the GB ‘A prayer to


[TD 169]

be said before meals’, i.e. the two verses, followed by: ‘O heavenly Father, which art the fountain and full treasure of all goodness, we beseech thee to show thy mercies upon us thy children, and sanctify these gifts which we receive of thy merciful liberality.’

3817-26 Combines the Psalm (117) from the GB ‘Thanksgiving after meals’ with the opening sentence of the BCO’s second ‘thanksgiving after meat’: ‘The God of glory and peace, who hath created, redeemed and presently fed us, be blessed for ever. So be it.’ The insertion of the Lord’s Prayer is Carswell’s own.

3827-35 From BCO: the first ‘thanksgiving before meat’ is passed over; 3828-35 represents the second: the verses from Psalm 145.15, 16, and ‘Good Lord, bless us and the gifts which we receive of thy large liberality through Jesus Christ our Lord. So be it. Our Father, &c.’

3836-47 The first paragraph of BCO ‘thanksgiving after meat’: ‘Glory, praise, and honour be unto thee, most merciful and omnipotent Father, who of thine infinite goodness hast created man to thine own image and similitude; who hast also fed, and daily feedest of thy most bountiful hand all living creatures, grant unto us that as thou hast nourished these our mortal bodies with corporal food, so thou wouldst replenish our souls with the perfect knowledge of the lively word of thy beloved Son Jesus, to whom be praise, glory, and honour, for ever. So be it.’ Carswell adds the Lord’s Prayer.

3849-61 BCO’s first ‘thanksgiving before meat’ abridged: ‘Eternal and everlasting God, Father of our Lord Jesus Christ, who of thy most singular love which thou bearest to mankind, hast appointed to his sustenance not only the fruits of the earth, but also the fowls of the air, and beasts of the earth, and fishes of the sea, and hast commanded thy benefits to be received as from thine hands with thanksgiving, assuring thy children by the mouth of thine Apostle, that to the clean all things are clean, as the creatures which be sanctified by the word and by prayer; grant unto us so moderately to use these thy gifts present, that the bodies being refreshed, the souls may be more able to proceed in all good works, to the praise of thine holy Name, through Jesus Christ our Lord. So be it. Our Father which art, &c.’

3859 <gai>umhal do ghluasacht:<eng> ‘more able to proceed’; but the sense of “ready, willing” is more usually conveyed by Carswell by using <gai>ullamh.<eng>


[TD 170]

3862-75 This grace begins with a different rendering of the opening of BCO’s second ‘thanksgiving after meat’, already used at 3824-5, and then follows the second part of its first ‘thanksgiving after meat’, the first part of which appears as 3837-47: ‘God save the Church universal; God comfort them that be comfortless; Lord, increase our faith. O Lord, for Christ thy Son’s sake, be merciful to the commonwealths where thy Gospel is truly preached and harbour granted to the afflicted members of Christ’s body, and illuminate according to thy good pleasure, all nations with the brightness of thy word. So be it.’

3877-3903 Apparently an original contribution by Carswell, though it may perhaps be a reformed version of an older formula.

3900 <gai>do chum chuain agas chalaidh: cuan<eng> here in the old sense of “harbour”, as shown by the parallel <gai>caladh,<eng> not in the Sc.G. and Manx sense of “sea”.

3904 On the poem cf. also CC p. 220. The English metrical psalms gave rise to similar metrical versions of the Prayerbook canticles and of the Lord’s Prayer and Commandments, and similarly in the 1575 edition of BCO the Lord’s Prayer and the Ten Commandments (for a version of which see CC p. 221-3) followed the metrical psalms. With the phrase <gai>a meadarthacht dhána<eng> (copied into CC iv) cf. CC i <gai>ina meadardhachd,<eng> and the Caogad’s <gai>a meadrachd Gaoidhilg<eng> and the Metrical Psalms (1694) <gai>a meadar dhana Gaoidheilg.<eng>

3917 <gai>Beatha na h-anma ’s an chuirp:<eng> printed <gai>sa chuirp,<eng> an early example of the loss of -n in the article before lenited consonants.

3920 <gai>gan dlaoi gan dáil:<eng> both phrases are present primarily for metrical reasons; <gai>dáil<eng> “delay, hesitation” is clear enough, but on <gai>dlaoi<eng> see CDIL s.v. dlaí. None of the meanings given there is very suitable and this example is not quoted; perhaps some further development of sense (b) fig. “screen, shelter” towards “obstacle” would fit. Professor Matheson suggested “without stint”.
