3927 <gai>a mbuaidhridh lind:<eng> Professor Matheson hesitated between this reading and <gai>ar mbuaidhridh,<eng> rendering “aid us against our temptations(?)”, i.e. taking lind with <gai>bí.<eng> It would be more natural to take lind with <gai>buaidhreadh,<eng> but then with <gai>ar<eng> we should need to have <gai>leó,<eng> i.e. “oppose our being troubled by them” (sc. <gai>ar náimhde<eng>), which is forbidden by the metre. If we leave the text alone we must assume <gai>buaidhreadh lé,<eng> “oppose their troubling us”, which fits rather better with <gai>ar a n-iocht<eng> “at their mercy”.


[TD 171]

3935 <gai>go ndénaid … sgige:<eng> the sense seems certainly future “will ridicule”, i.e. when it comes into their hands, but -dénaid is formally present, and Carswell has no examples of the dependent future stem diongn- at all (cf. CC <gai>le ndiongantar<eng> 126 b 7). Similarly at 3944.

3938 <gai>cruas:<eng> “hardness”, but as a laudatory stylistic term not “obscurity” but “vigour, firmness”. Professor Matheson suggests “precision”.

3939 <gai>foclōracht:<eng> the word is not noted in DIL, nor is <gai>foclóir,<eng> which occurs in Lhuyd as “a vocabulary”; Carswell’s word may be an earlier attempt at creating a term with this meaning, or, assuming an unrecorded foclóir, he may have wished to distinguish between “a vocabulary” and “vocabulary”. The sentence seems to refer to (a) deficiencies in his vocabulary or command of appropriate terms, (b) want of correctness in writing, i.e. presumably in spelling, and (c) misprints, which are not entirely his own responsibility. In the last phrase <gai>litre<eng> appears to be gen. sg. dependent on <gai>uireasbhuidh<eng> like <gai>fhoclōrachta<eng> and <gai>chearta;<eng> the sense might be clearer if we read <gai>liter a n-inadh litre ele<eng> “one letter in place of another”. The pl. <gai>litre a n-inadh litreadh ele<eng> is also possible. For <gai>litear,<eng> not <gai>litir,<eng> see IGT Decl. § 54.

3942 <gai>do thuairim:<eng> cf. CDIL s.v. túaraim, where there is no exact parallel to this phrase. The alternative <gai>do bharamhail<eng> is “by guess”, and so probably here “by likelihood”, with reference to the compositor’s having guessed at the likeliest interpretation of the ambiguities of Carswell’s handwriting.

3967-70 This quatrain was first identified by Professor W. J. Watson, Trans. of the Gaelic Society of Inverness XXX 290, as coming from a poem by Tadhg Og O hUiginn (ob. 1448), beginning Mairg danab soirbh an saoghal, edited by Fr. Lambert MacKenna in his Dán Dé, p. 13. Professor Matheson translates: Woe to him with whom the world prospers, woe to him who should obtain its favour; if our tie be to the world there is danger that God’s will remains undone.

3971-2 This couplet comes from a poem by Fearghal mac Domhnuill Ruaidh mac an Bhaird, published as no. 48 in his Dioghluim Dána by Fr. MacKenna, stanza 28cd. It is cited in IGT Decl. § 74, line 1572, for the dat. sg. cronn. The poem begins Olc íocthar ar luagh leighis. Professor Matheson translates: He has put


[TD 172]

with this spear of the breast a protecting hoop round the blood of Eve. For <gai>cíoch, cíogh<eng> m., with gen. sg. <gai>cíogha,<eng> see IGT Decl. § 46.

3979-80 This couplet is from the same poem as the preceding, stanza 13cd: The grace of God—it is in its beginning we are—it has not yet reached an end.

[TD 183-186]

APPENDIX II
[Beurla]


[TD 187]

GLOSSARY

The Glossary lists the whole vocabulary of the text with the exception of the forms of the irregular verbs, which are collected together in the Introduction, section II (B). Elements occurring only after certain prefixes, and proper names, are separately listed. The intention has been to include all variant spellings and inflected forms. Where variant spellings of the same word would significantly affect the alphabetical order the headword conforms to the spelling of the first instance noted, whether or not this turns out to be the commonest spelling, and cross-references are added. Corrections to the spelling and accentuation of the original which have been adopted in the text appear also in the Glossary, though a few abnormally accented forms are also admitted. Mutations, other than permanent ones, are removed in quoting forms.

Inflected forms of verbs are entered under the verbnoun where this occurs or can reasonably be inferred; in a few instances where Carswell does not use the verbnoun and elsewhere more than one form occurs, the stem is used as headword. The strictly speaking superfluous description “vbn. and n. m.” is used to make clear that the verbnoun occurs not only in verbal contexts (as with ag and do) but also in nominal ones (as with the article and in the plural). The terms “conditional” and “impersonal” have been adopted in preference to “secondary future” and “passive”.

Nouns and adjectives are arranged under the nominative singular (if there is no reference this is an inferred form); in the singular the dative is noticed as a separate case only when it has a distinct form, and the accusative only when it has a distinct form and occurs as the direct object of an inflected tense. Where gender is proved by instances in the text or can reasonably be inferred it is stated; where it is not so proved and usage varies or is uncertain “mf.” or simply “n.” is recorded.

In the case of prepositions the combinations with the article, the possessive particles, the relative, and the personal pronouns are


[TD 188]

all entered under the simple preposition. Phrase-prepositions are placed under their nominal element.

The explanations are based on the English which Carswell was translating, but as he does not usually render word for word, and as changes in the meaning of English words would make some glosses too closely based on the original misleading, more modern terms have in many cases been supplied or substituted.

1. <gai>a<eng> (len.) voc. part. 43, 50, 133.

2. <gai>a<eng> gen. 3rd pers. pron.; (len.) sg. m. 93, 124-5, 154; f. 142, 274, <gai>a h-<eng> 486, 1998; (nas.) 72, 100, 104.
